Configuration
[![Image 4: The components of the ISS in an exploded diagram, with modules on-orbit highlighted in orange.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/ISS_configuration_2022-12_en.svg/250px-ISS_configuration_2022-12_en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_configuration_2022-12_en.svg)

Station elements as of December 2022

([exploded view](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploded-view_drawing "Exploded-view drawing"))

The **International Space Station** (**ISS**) is a large [space station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_station "Space station") that was [assembled](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assembly_of_the_International_Space_Station "Assembly of the International Space Station") and is maintained in [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit") by a collaboration of five space agencies and their contractors: [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") (United States), [Roscosmos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roscosmos "Roscosmos") (Russia), [ESA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency") (Europe), [JAXA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JAXA "JAXA") (Japan), and [CSA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency "Canadian Space Agency") (Canada). As the largest space station ever constructed, it primarily serves as a platform for conducting scientific experiments in [microgravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microgravity "Microgravity") and studying the [space environment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_environment "Space environment").[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-11)

The station is divided into two main sections: the [Russian Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment "Russian Orbital Segment") (ROS), developed by Roscosmos, and the [US Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment "US Orbital Segment") (USOS), built by NASA, ESA, JAXA, and CSA. A striking feature of the ISS is the [Integrated Truss Structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure "Integrated Truss Structure"), which connect the station’s vast system of [solar panels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_panel "Solar panel") and [radiators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacecraft_thermal_control "Spacecraft thermal control") to its pressurized modules. These modules support diverse functions, including scientific research, crew habitation, storage, spacecraft control, and airlock operations. The ISS has eight [docking and berthing ports](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft "Docking and berthing of spacecraft") for visiting spacecraft. The station orbits the Earth at an average altitude of 400 kilometres (250 miles)[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-12) and circles the Earth in roughly 93 minutes, completing 15.5 orbits per day.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tracking2-13)

The [ISS programme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station_programme "International Space Station programme") combines two previously planned crewed Earth-orbiting stations: the United States' [Space Station _Freedom_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Station_Freedom "Space Station Freedom") and the Soviet Union's _[Mir-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir-2 "Mir-2")_. The [first ISS module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zarya_(ISS_module) "Zarya (ISS module)") was launched in 1998, with major components delivered by [Proton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton_(rocket_family) "Proton (rocket family)") and [Soyuz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_(rocket_family) "Soyuz (rocket family)") rockets and the [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle"). Long-term occupancy began on 2 November 2000, with the arrival of the [Expedition 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_1 "Expedition 1") crew. Since then, the ISS has remained continuously inhabited for 24 years and 288 days, the longest continuous human presence in space. As of August 2025, 290 individuals from 26 countries had visited the station.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-14)

Future plans for the ISS include the addition of at least one module, [Axiom Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Space "Axiom Space")'s [Payload Power Thermal Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Station "Axiom Station"). The station is expected to remain operational until the end of 2030, after which it will be de-orbited using [a dedicated NASA spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Deorbit_Vehicle "US Deorbit Vehicle").[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:2b-15)

Conception
----------

As the [space race](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_race "Space race") drew to a close in the early 1970s, the US and [USSR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/USSR "USSR") began to contemplate a variety of potential collaborations in outer space. This culminated in the 1975 [Apollo-Soyuz Test Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo-Soyuz_Test_Project "Apollo-Soyuz Test Project"), the first docking of spacecraft from two different spacefaring nations. The ASTP was considered a success, and further joint missions were also contemplated.

One such concept was International Skylab, which proposed launching the backup [Skylab B](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skylab_B "Skylab B") space station for a mission that would see multiple visits by both [Apollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_CSM "Apollo CSM") and [Soyuz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft) "Soyuz (spacecraft)") crew vehicles.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-16) More ambitious was the Skylab-Salyut Space Laboratory, which proposed docking the Skylab B to a Soviet [Salyut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salyut_programme "Salyut programme") space station. Falling budgets and rising [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War") tensions in the late 1970s saw these concepts fall by the wayside, along with another plan to have the [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle") dock with a Salyut space station.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Space_Station_programme_wired-20120326-17)

In the early 1980s, [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") planned to launch a modular space station called _Freedom_ as a counterpart to the Salyut and _[Mir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir "Mir")_ space stations. In 1984 the ESA was invited to participate in Space Station _Freedom_, and the ESA approved the Columbus laboratory by 1987.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Space_Station_programme_ESA_-_Columbus-18) The [Japanese Experiment Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module "Japanese Experiment Module") (JEM), or _Kibō_, was announced in 1985, as part of the _Freedom_ space station in response to a NASA request in 1982.

In early 1985, science ministers from the [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency") (ESA) countries approved the _Columbus_ programme, the most ambitious effort in space undertaken by that organization at the time. The plan spearheaded by Germany and Italy included a module which would be attached to _Freedom_, and with the capability to evolve into a full-fledged European orbital outpost before the end of the century.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Space_Station_programme_astronautix.com-19)

Increasing costs threw these plans into doubt in the early 1990s. Congress was unwilling to provide enough money to build and operate _Freedom_, and demanded NASA increase international participation to defray the rising costs or they would cancel the entire project outright.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Space_Station_programme_nyt-19930608-20)

Simultaneously, the USSR was conducting planning for the [Mir-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir-2 "Mir-2") space station, and had begun constructing modules for the new station by the mid-1980s. However the [collapse of the Soviet Union](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Soviet_Union "Collapse of the Soviet Union") required these plans to be greatly downscaled, and soon Mir-2 was in danger of never being launched at all.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-21) With both space station projects in jeopardy, American and Russian officials met and proposed they be combined. [[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-22)

In September 1993, American Vice-President [Al Gore](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al_Gore_Jr. "Al Gore Jr.") and Russian Prime Minister [Viktor Chernomyrdin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viktor_Chernomyrdin "Viktor Chernomyrdin") announced plans for a new space station, which eventually became the International Space Station.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Space_Station_programme_gao-23) They also agreed, in preparation for this new project, that the United States would be involved in the Mir programme, including American Shuttles docking, in the [Shuttle–_Mir_ programme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shuttle%E2%80%93Mir_program "Shuttle–Mir program").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Space_Station_programme_SMB-24)

Purpose
-------

The ISS was originally intended to be a laboratory, observatory, and factory while providing transportation, maintenance, and a [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit") staging base for possible future missions to the Moon, Mars, and asteroids. However, not all of the uses envisioned in the initial [memorandum of understanding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Memorandum_of_understanding "Memorandum of understanding") between [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") and [Roscosmos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roscosmos "Roscosmos") have been realised.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-RSA-MOU-25) In the [2010 United States National Space Policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_policy_of_the_Barack_Obama_administration "Space policy of the Barack Obama administration"), the ISS was given additional roles of serving commercial, diplomatic,[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-payette-2012-26) and educational purposes.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-USNSP-27)

### Scientific research

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg/120px-Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg)

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg/250px-ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg)

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Space_Shuttle_Endeavour.jpg/250px-STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Space_Shuttle_Endeavour.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Space_Shuttle_Endeavour.jpg)

Fisheye view of several labs and the Space Shuttle

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg/250px-NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg)

The ISS provides a platform to conduct scientific research, with power, data, cooling, and crew available to support experiments. Small uncrewed spacecraft can also provide platforms for experiments, especially those involving zero gravity and exposure to space, but space stations offer a long-term environment where studies can be performed potentially for decades, combined with ready access by human researchers.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-10th-28)[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Worldbook_at_NASA-29)

The ISS simplifies individual experiments by allowing groups of experiments to share the same launches and crew time. Research is conducted in a wide variety of fields, including [astrobiology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrobiology "Astrobiology"), [astronomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy "Astronomy"), [physical sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_science "Physical science"), [materials science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materials_science "Materials science"), [space weather](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_weather "Space weather"), [meteorology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology "Meteorology"), and [human research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zero_g#Health_effects_of_weightlessness "Zero g") including [space medicine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_medicine "Space medicine") and the [life sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_science "Life science").[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-30)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_ISS_Goals-31)[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-32)[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa-20110311-33) Scientists on Earth have timely access to the data and can suggest experimental modifications to the crew. If follow-on experiments are necessary, the routinely scheduled launches of resupply craft allows new hardware to be launched with relative ease.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Worldbook_at_NASA-29) Crews fly [expeditions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_expeditions "List of International Space Station expeditions") of several months' duration, providing approximately 160 man-hours per week of labour with a crew of six. However, a considerable amount of crew time is taken up by station maintenance.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Science_in_School-34)

Perhaps the most notable ISS experiment is the [Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer "Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer") (AMS), which is intended to detect dark matter and answer other fundamental questions about our universe. According to NASA, the AMS is as important as the [Hubble Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope "Hubble Space Telescope"). Currently docked on station, it could not have been easily accommodated on a free flying satellite platform because of its power and bandwidth needs.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20110318-35)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20090814-36) On 3 April 2013, scientists reported that hints of [dark matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter "Dark matter") may have been detected by the AMS.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-APS-20130403-37)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-AMS-20130403-38)[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-AP-20130403-39)[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-BBC-20130403-40)[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA-20130403-41)[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NYT-20130403-42) According to the scientists, "The first results from the space-borne Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer confirm an unexplained excess of high-energy positrons in Earth-bound cosmic rays".[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-43)

The space environment is hostile to life. Unprotected presence in space is characterised by an intense radiation field (consisting primarily of protons and other subatomic charged particles from the [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind"), in addition to [cosmic rays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_ray "Cosmic ray")), high vacuum, extreme temperatures, and microgravity.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Space_Microbiology-44) Some simple forms of life called [extremophiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extremophile "Extremophile"),[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Beer_microbes-45) as well as small invertebrates called [tardigrades](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tardigrade "Tardigrade")[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Waterbears-46) can survive in this environment in an extremely dry state through [desiccation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desiccation#Biology_and_ecology "Desiccation").

Medical research improves knowledge about the effects of long-term space exposure on the human body, including [muscle atrophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muscle_atrophy "Muscle atrophy"), [bone loss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osteoporosis "Osteoporosis"), and fluid shift. These data will be used to determine whether high duration [human spaceflight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_spaceflight "Human spaceflight") and [space colonisation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_colonisation "Space colonisation") are feasible. In 2006, data on bone loss and muscular atrophy suggested that there would be a significant risk of fractures and movement problems if astronauts landed on a planet after a lengthy interplanetary cruise, such as the six-month interval required to [travel to Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars "Human mission to Mars").[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-JCB-47)[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-newscientist-20090722-48)

Medical studies are conducted aboard the ISS on behalf of the [National Space Biomedical Research Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Space_Biomedical_Research_Institute "National Space Biomedical Research Institute") (NSBRI). Prominent among these is the [Advanced Diagnostic Ultrasound in Microgravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Diagnostic_Ultrasound_in_Microgravity "Advanced Diagnostic Ultrasound in Microgravity") study in which astronauts perform ultrasound scans under the guidance of remote experts. The study considers the diagnosis and treatment of medical conditions in space. Usually, there is no physician on board the ISS and diagnosis of medical conditions is a challenge. It is anticipated that remotely guided ultrasound scans will have application on Earth in emergency and rural care situations where access to a trained physician is difficult.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-adum-49)[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-50)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-51)

In August 2020, scientists reported that [bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria") from Earth, particularly _[Deinococcus radiodurans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deinococcus\_radiodurans "Deinococcus radiodurans")_ bacteria, which is highly resistant to [environmental hazards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Environmental_hazard "Environmental hazard"), were found to survive for three years in [outer space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_space "Outer space"), based on studies conducted on the International Space Station. These findings supported the notion of [panspermia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panspermia "Panspermia"), the hypothesis that [life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life "Life") exists throughout the [Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe"), distributed in various ways, including [space dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_dust "Space dust"), [meteoroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteoroid "Meteoroid"), [asteroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid "Asteroid"), [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet "Comet"), [planetoids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minor_planet "Minor planet") or [contaminated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contamination "Contamination")[spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacecraft "Spacecraft").[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-CNN-20200826-52)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-FM-20200826-53)

[Remote sensing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Remote_sensing "Remote sensing") of the Earth, astronomy, and deep space research on the ISS have significantly increased during the 2010s after the completion of the [US Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment "US Orbital Segment") in 2011. Throughout the more than 20 years of the ISS program, researchers aboard the ISS and on the ground have examined [aerosols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerosol "Aerosol"), [ozone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ozone "Ozone"), [lightning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lightning "Lightning"), and [oxides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxide "Oxide") in Earth's atmosphere, as well as the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun"), cosmic rays, [cosmic dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_dust "Cosmic dust"), [antimatter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antimatter "Antimatter"), and dark matter in the universe. Examples of Earth-viewing remote sensing experiments that have flown on the ISS are the [Orbiting Carbon Observatory 3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiting_Carbon_Observatory_3 "Orbiting Carbon Observatory 3"), [ISS-RapidScat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISS-RapidScat "ISS-RapidScat"), [ECOSTRESS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ECOSTRESS "ECOSTRESS"), the [Global Ecosystem Dynamics Investigation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Ecosystem_Dynamics_Investigation "Global Ecosystem Dynamics Investigation"), and the [Cloud Aerosol Transport System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloud_Aerosol_Transport_System "Cloud Aerosol Transport System"). ISS-based astronomy telescopes and experiments include [SOLAR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SOLAR_(ISS) "SOLAR (ISS)"), the [Neutron Star Interior Composition Explorer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_Star_Interior_Composition_Explorer "Neutron Star Interior Composition Explorer"), the [Calorimetric Electron Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calorimetric_Electron_Telescope "Calorimetric Electron Telescope"), the [Monitor of All-sky X-ray Image (MAXI)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MAXI_(ISS_experiment) "MAXI (ISS experiment)"), and the [Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer "Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer").[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-30)

#### Freefall

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg/250px-ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg)

ISS crew member storing samples

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Space_Fire.jpg/250px-Space_Fire.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Space_Fire.jpg)

A comparison between the combustion of a candle on [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth") (left) and in a free fall environment, such as that found on the ISS (right)

Researchers are investigating the effect of the station's near-weightless environment on the evolution, development, growth and internal processes of plants and animals. In response to some of the data, NASA wants to investigate [microgravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microgravity "Microgravity")'s effects on the growth of three-dimensional, human-like tissues and the unusual [protein crystals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein_crystal "Protein crystal") that can be formed in space.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-30)

Investigating the physics of fluids in microgravity will provide better models of the behaviour of fluids. Because fluids can be almost completely combined in microgravity, physicists investigate fluids that do not mix well on Earth. Examining reactions that are slowed by low gravity and low temperatures will improve our understanding of [superconductivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superconductivity "Superconductivity").[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-30)

The study of [materials science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materials_science "Materials science") is an important ISS research activity, with the objective of reaping economic benefits through the improvement of techniques used on Earth.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-materials-science-101-55) Other areas of interest include the effect of low gravity on combustion, through the study of the efficiency of burning and control of emissions and pollutants. These findings may improve knowledge about energy production and lead to economic and environmental benefits.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-30)

### Exploration

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Mars500.jpg/330px-Mars500.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mars500.jpg)

A 3D plan of the Russia-based [MARS-500](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MARS-500 "MARS-500") complex, used for conducting ground-based experiments that complement ISS-based preparations for a [human mission to Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars "Human mission to Mars")

The ISS provides a location in the relative safety of low Earth orbit to test spacecraft systems that will be required for long-duration missions to the Moon and Mars. This provides experience in operations, maintenance, and repair and replacement activities on-orbit. This will help develop essential skills in operating spacecraft farther from Earth, reduce mission risks, and advance the capabilities of interplanetary spacecraft.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ResProg-56) Referring to the [MARS-500](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MARS-500 "MARS-500") experiment, a crew isolation experiment conducted on Earth, ESA states, "Whereas the ISS is essential for answering questions concerning the possible impact of weightlessness, radiation and other space-specific factors, aspects such as the effect of long-term isolation and confinement can be more appropriately addressed via ground-based simulations".[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa_mars500-57) Sergey Krasnov, the head of human space flight programmes for Russia's space agency, Roscosmos, in 2011 suggested a "shorter version" of MARS-500 may be carried out on the ISS.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Mars_thing_on_ISS-58)

In 2009, noting the value of the partnership framework itself, Sergey Krasnov wrote, "When compared with partners acting separately, partners developing complementary abilities and resources could give us much more assurance of the success and safety of space exploration. The ISS is helping further advance near-Earth space exploration and realisation of prospective programmes of research and exploration of the Solar system, including the Moon and Mars."[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-IAF2009-59)[A crewed mission to Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars "Human mission to Mars") may be a multinational effort involving space agencies and countries outside the current ISS partnership. In 2010, ESA Director-General Jean-Jacques Dordain stated his agency was ready to propose to the other four partners that China, India, and South Korea be invited to join the ISS partnership.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ESAproposesInvite-60) NASA chief [Charles Bolden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Bolden "Charles Bolden") stated in February 2011, "Any mission to Mars is likely to be a global effort."[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Mars_a_global_effort-61) Currently, [US federal legislation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolf_Amendment "Wolf Amendment") prevents NASA co-operation with China on space projects without approval by the [FBI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FBI "FBI") and Congress.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-justice1-62)

### Education and cultural outreach

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg/250px-Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg)

Original [Jules Verne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jules_Verne "Jules Verne") manuscripts displayed by crew inside the [_Jules Verne_ ATV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jules_Verne_ATV "Jules Verne ATV") (Automated Transfer Vehicle)

The ISS crew provides opportunities for students on Earth by running student-developed experiments, making educational demonstrations, allowing for student participation in classroom versions of ISS experiments, and directly engaging students using radio, and email.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSRG-63)[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-64) ESA offers a wide range of free teaching materials that can be downloaded for use in classrooms.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa-education-material-65) In one lesson, students can navigate a [3D model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/3D_modeling "3D modeling") of the interior and exterior of the ISS, and face spontaneous challenges to solve in real time.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa-iss-3d-66)

The [Japanese Aerospace Exploration Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency "Japanese Aerospace Exploration Agency") (JAXA) aims to inspire children to "pursue craftsmanship" and to heighten their "awareness of the importance of life and their responsibilities in society".[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-jaxa-peaceinyounggminds-67) Through a series of education guides, students develop a deeper understanding of the past and near-term future of crewed space flight, as well as that of Earth and life.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-jaxa-sunflower-seeds-68)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-jaxa-asagao-69) In the JAXA "Seeds in Space" experiments, the mutation effects of spaceflight on plant seeds aboard the ISS are explored by growing sunflower seeds that have flown on the ISS for about nine months. In the first phase of _Kibō_ utilisation from 2008 to mid-2010, researchers from more than a dozen Japanese universities conducted experiments in diverse fields.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-70)

Cultural activities are another major objective of the ISS programme. Tetsuo Tanaka, the director of JAXA's Space Environment and Utilization Center, has said: "There is something about space that touches even people who are not interested in science."[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-jaxa2-71)

[Amateur Radio on the ISS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amateur_Radio_on_the_International_Space_Station "Amateur Radio on the International Space Station") (ARISS) is a volunteer programme that encourages students worldwide to pursue careers in science, technology, engineering, and mathematics, through [amateur radio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amateur_radio "Amateur radio") communications opportunities with the ISS crew. ARISS is an international working group, consisting of delegations from nine countries including several in Europe, as well as Japan, Russia, Canada, and the United States. In areas where radio equipment cannot be used, [speakerphones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speakerphone "Speakerphone") connect students to ground stations which then connect the calls to the space station.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-72)

Spoken voice recording by ESA astronaut [Paolo Nespoli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paolo_Nespoli "Paolo Nespoli") on the subject of the ISS, produced in November 2017 for Wikipedia

_[First Orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First\_Orbit "First Orbit")_ is a 2011 feature-length documentary film about [Vostok 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vostok_1 "Vostok 1"), the first crewed space flight around the Earth. By matching the orbit of the ISS to that of Vostok 1 as closely as possible, in terms of ground path and time of day, documentary filmmaker [Christopher Riley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Riley "Christopher Riley") and ESA astronaut [Paolo Nespoli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paolo_Nespoli "Paolo Nespoli") were able to film the view that [Yuri Gagarin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin "Yuri Gagarin") saw on his pioneering orbital space flight. This new footage was cut together with the original Vostok 1 mission audio recordings sourced from the Russian State Archive. Nespoli is credited as the [director of photography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cinematographer "Cinematographer") for this documentary film, as he recorded the majority of the footage himself during [Expedition 26](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_26 "Expedition 26")/[27](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_27 "Expedition 27").[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-guardian-20110411-73) The film was streamed in a global YouTube premiere in 2011 under a free licence through the website _firstorbit.org_.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-firstorbit-faq-74)

In May 2013, commander [Chris Hadfield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chris_Hadfield "Chris Hadfield") shot a music video of [David Bowie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Bowie "David Bowie")'s "[Space Oddity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Oddity "Space Oddity")" on board the station, which was released on YouTube.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-75)[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-bbc-20130513-76) It was the first music video filmed in space.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-gizmodo-20130512-77)

In November 2017, while participating in [Expedition 52](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_52 "Expedition 52")/[53](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_53 "Expedition 53") on the ISS, [Paolo Nespoli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paolo_Nespoli "Paolo Nespoli") made two recordings of his spoken voice (one in English and the other in his native Italian), for use on [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia "Wikipedia") articles. These were the first content made in space specifically for Wikipedia.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Mabbett-78)[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Petris-79)

In November 2021, a [virtual reality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virtual_reality "Virtual reality") exhibit called The Infinite featuring life aboard the ISS was announced.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacecom-20211123-80)

International co-operation
--------------------------

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/ISS_Agreements.jpg/250px-ISS_Agreements.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_Agreements.jpg)

A Commemorative Plaque honouring Space Station Intergovernmental Agreement signed on 28 January 1998

Involving five space programs and fifteen countries,[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Cooperation-81) the International Space Station is the most politically and legally complex space exploration programme in history.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-International_Cooperation-81) The 1998 Space Station Intergovernmental Agreement sets forth the primary framework for international cooperation among the parties. A series of subsequent agreements govern other aspects of the station, ranging from jurisdictional issues to a code of conduct among visiting astronauts.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-farand-iss-framework-82)

Brazil was also invited to participate in the programme, the only developing country to receive such an invitation. Under the agreement framework, Brazil was to provide six pieces of hardware, and in exchange, would receive ISS utilization rights. However, Brazil was unable to deliver any of the elements due to a lack of funding and political priority within the country. Brazil officially dropped out of the ISS programme in 2007.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-83)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-84)

Following the [2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine "2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine"), continued cooperation between Russia and other countries on the International Space Station has been put into question. Roscosmos Director General [Dmitry Rogozin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmitry_Rogozin "Dmitry Rogozin") insinuated that Russian withdrawal could cause the International Space Station to de-orbit due to lack of reboost capabilities, writing in a series of tweets, "If you block cooperation with us, who will save the ISS from an unguided de-orbit to impact on the territory of the US or Europe? There's also the chance of impact of the 500-ton construction in India or China. Do you want to threaten them with such a prospect? The ISS doesn't fly over Russia, so all the risk is yours. Are you ready for it?"[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-arstechnica-20220225-85) (This latter claim is untrue: the ISS flies over all parts of the Earth between 51.6 degrees latitude north and south, approximately the latitude of [Saratov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saratov "Saratov").) Rogozin later tweeted that normal relations between ISS partners could only be restored once sanctions have been lifted, and indicated that Roscosmos would submit proposals to the Russian government on ending cooperation.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-arstechnica-20220402-86) NASA stated that, if necessary, US corporation [Northrop Grumman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northrop_Grumman "Northrop Grumman") has offered a reboost capability that would keep the ISS in orbit.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-guardian-20220301-87)

On 26 July 2022, [Yury Borisov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yury_Borisov "Yury Borisov"), Rogozin's successor as head of Roscosmos, submitted to Russian President Putin plans for withdrawal from the programme after 2024.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-26JulyBorisovQuote-88) However, Robyn Gatens, the NASA official in charge of the space station, responded that NASA had not received any formal notices from Roscosmos concerning withdrawal plans.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Roulette_2022-89)

### Participating countries

Construction
------------

### Manufacturing

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/SSPF_interior.jpg/250px-SSPF_interior.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SSPF_interior.jpg)

[_Harmony_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmony_(ISS_module) "Harmony (ISS module)") in the [Space Station Processing Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Systems_Processing_Facility "Space Systems Processing Facility")

The International Space Station is a product of global collaboration, with its components manufactured across the world.

The modules of the [Russian Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment "Russian Orbital Segment"), including _[Zarya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zarya\_(ISS\_module) "Zarya (ISS module)")_ and _[Zvezda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zvezda\_(ISS\_module) "Zvezda (ISS module)")_, were produced at the [Khrunichev State Research and Production Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Khrunichev_State_Research_and_Production_Space_Center "Khrunichev State Research and Production Space Center") in Moscow. _Zvezda_ was initially manufactured in 1985 as a component for the _[Mir-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir-2 "Mir-2")_ space station, which was never launched.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:4-90)[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-91)

Much of the [US Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment "US Orbital Segment"), including the _[Destiny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Destiny\_(ISS\_module) "Destiny (ISS module)")_ and _[Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unity\_(ISS\_module) "Unity (ISS module)")_ modules, the [Integrated Truss Structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure "Integrated Truss Structure"), and [solar arrays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_system_of_the_International_Space_Station "Electrical system of the International Space Station"), were built at NASA's [Marshall Space Flight Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center "Marshall Space Flight Center") in [Huntsville, Alabama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama "Huntsville, Alabama") and [Michoud Assembly Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility "Michoud Assembly Facility") in [New Orleans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Orleans "New Orleans").[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:4-90) These components underwent final assembly and processing for launch at the [Operations and Checkout Building](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operations_and_Checkout_Building "Operations and Checkout Building") and the [Space Station Processing Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Station_Processing_Facility "Space Station Processing Facility") (SSPF) at the [Kennedy Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center "Kennedy Space Center") in Florida.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20160219-92)

The US Orbital Segment also hosts the [_Columbus_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbus_(ISS_module) "Columbus (ISS module)") module contributed by the [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency") and built in Germany, the [_Kibō_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kib%C5%8D_(ISS_module) "Kibō (ISS module)") module contributed by [Japan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JAXA "JAXA") and built at the [Tsukuba Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsukuba_Space_Center "Tsukuba Space Center") and the [Institute of Space and Astronautical Science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institute_of_Space_and_Astronautical_Science "Institute of Space and Astronautical Science"), along with the [Canadarm2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadarm2 "Canadarm2") and [Dextre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dextre "Dextre"), a joint Canadian-U.S. endeavor. All of these components were shipped to the SSPF for launch processing.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:4-90)[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-img-ksc-08pd0991-93)

### Assembly

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/ISS-assembly-animation.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-assembly-animation.gif)

Animation of the [assembly of the International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assembly_of_the_International_Space_Station "Assembly of the International Space Station")

The assembly of the International Space Station, a major endeavour in [space architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_architecture "Space architecture"), began in November 1998.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-OnOrbit-8)

Modules in the Russian segment launched and docked autonomously, with the exception of _[Rassvet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rassvet\_(ISS\_module) "Rassvet (ISS module)")_. Other modules and components were delivered by the [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle"), which then had to be installed by astronauts either remotely using robotic arms or during spacewalks, more formally known as [extra-vehicular activities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extravehicular_activity "Extravehicular activity") (EVAs). By 5 June 2011 astronauts had made over 159 EVAs to add components to the station, totaling more than 1,000 hours in space.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISStD-94)[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-mcc-95)

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/ISS_from_Atlantis_-_Sts101-714-016.jpg/250px-ISS_from_Atlantis_-_Sts101-714-016.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_from_Atlantis_-_Sts101-714-016.jpg)

_Zarya_ and _Unity_, the first two modules of the ISS, pictured in May 2000

The beginning of the core of the ISS's tenure in orbit was the launch of the Russian-built _[Zarya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zarya\_(ISS\_module) "Zarya (ISS module)")_ module atop a [Proton rocket](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton_(rocket_family) "Proton (rocket family)") on 20 November 1998. _Zarya_ provided propulsion, [attitude control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacecraft_attitude_control "Spacecraft attitude control"), communications, and electrical power. Two weeks later on 4 December 1998, the American-made _[Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unity\_(ISS\_module) "Unity (ISS module)")_ was ferried aboard [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Endeavour_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour "Space Shuttle Endeavour") on [STS-88](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-88 "STS-88") and joined with _Zarya_. _Unity_ provided the connection between the Russian and US segments of the station and would provide ports to connect future modules and visiting spacecraft.

While the connection of two modules built on different continents, by nations that were once bitter rivals was a significant milestone, these two initial modules lacked life support systems and the ISS remained unmanned for the next two years. At the time, the Russian station _[Mir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir "Mir")_ was still inhabited.

The turning point arrived in July 2000 with the launch of the _[Zvezda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zvezda\_(ISS\_module) "Zvezda (ISS module)")_ module. Equipped with living quarters and life-support systems, _Zvezda_ enabled continuous human presence aboard the station. The first crew, [Expedition 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_1 "Expedition 1"), arrived that November aboard [Soyuz TM-31](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_TM-31 "Soyuz TM-31").[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-96)[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-97)

The ISS grew steadily over the following years, with modules delivered by both Russian rockets and the Space Shuttle.

Expedition 1 arrived midway between the Space Shuttle flights of missions [STS-92](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-92 "STS-92") and [STS-97](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-97 "STS-97"). These two flights each added segments of the station's [Integrated Truss Structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure "Integrated Truss Structure"), which provided the station with [Ku band](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ku_band "Ku band") communications, additional attitude control needed for the additional mass of the USOS, and additional solar arrays.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-98) Over the next two years, the station continued to expand. A [Soyuz-U](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz-U "Soyuz-U") rocket delivered the [_Pirs_ docking compartment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pirs_(ISS_module) "Pirs (ISS module)"). The Space Shuttles _[Discovery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space\_Shuttle\_Discovery "Space Shuttle Discovery")_, _[Atlantis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space\_Shuttle\_Atlantis "Space Shuttle Atlantis")_, and _[Endeavour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space\_Shuttle\_Endeavour "Space Shuttle Endeavour")_ delivered the American _[Destiny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Destiny\_(ISS\_module) "Destiny (ISS module)")_ laboratory and [_Quest_ airlock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quest_Joint_Airlock "Quest Joint Airlock"), in addition to the station's main robot arm, the [Canadarm2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadarm2 "Canadarm2"), and several more segments of the Integrated Truss Structure.

Tragedy struck in 2003 with the [loss of the Space Shuttle _Columbia_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster "Space Shuttle Columbia disaster"), which grounded the rest of the Shuttle fleet, halting construction of the ISS.

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg/250px-International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg)

The ISS as seen from Space Shuttle Atlantis during [STS-132](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-132 "STS-132"), pictured in May 2010

Assembly resumed in 2006 with the arrival of [STS-115](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-115 "STS-115") with _Atlantis_, which delivered the station's second set of solar arrays. Several more truss segments and a third set of arrays were delivered on [STS-116](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-116 "STS-116"), [STS-117](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-117 "STS-117"), and [STS-118](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-118 "STS-118"). As a result of the major expansion of the station's power-generating capabilities, more modules could be accommodated, and the US _[Harmony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmony\_(ISS\_module) "Harmony (ISS module)")_ module and _[Columbus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbus\_(ISS\_module) "Columbus (ISS module)")_ European laboratory were added. These were soon followed by the first two components of the Japanese _[Kibō](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kibo\_(ISS\_module) "Kibo (ISS module)")_ laboratory. In March 2009, [STS-119](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-119 "STS-119") completed the Integrated Truss Structure with the installation of the fourth and final set of solar arrays. The final section of _Kibō_ was delivered in July 2009 on [STS-127](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-127 "STS-127"), followed by the Russian _[Poisk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisk\_(ISS\_module) "Poisk (ISS module)")_ module. The US _[Tranquility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tranquility\_(ISS\_module) "Tranquility (ISS module)")_ module was delivered in February 2010 during [STS-130](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-130 "STS-130"), alongside the _[Cupola](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupola\_(ISS\_module) "Cupola (ISS module)")_, followed by the penultimate Russian module, _[Rassvet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rassvet\_(ISS\_module) "Rassvet (ISS module)")_, in May 2010. _Rassvet_ was delivered by Space Shuttle _Atlantis_ on [STS-132](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-132 "STS-132") in exchange for the Russian Proton delivery of the US-funded _Zarya_ module in 1998.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-99) The last pressurised module of the USOS, _[Leonardo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonardo\_(ISS\_module) "Leonardo (ISS module)")_, was brought to the station in February 2011 on the final flight of _Discovery_, [STS-133](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-133 "STS-133").[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-sts-133-100)

Russia's new primary research module _Nauka_ docked in July 2021,[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tass-20210928-101) along with the European Robotic Arm which can relocate itself to different parts of the Russian modules of the station.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tass-20190325-102) Russia's latest addition, the [_Prichal_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prichal_(ISS_module) "Prichal (ISS module)") module, docked in November 2021.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-roscomos-20211126-103)

As of June 2025, nasa.gov states that there are 43 different modules and elements installed on the ISS.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISS_component_number-104)

Structure
---------

The ISS functions as a modular space station, enabling the addition or removal of modules from its structure for increased adaptability.

*   [![Image 18: Blueprint of ISS (as of 2018)](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/ISS_blueprint.png/960px-ISS_blueprint.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_blueprint.png "Blueprint of ISS (as of 2018)")

Blueprint of ISS (as of 2018)

*   [![Image 19: Rendering of ISS (as of 2023)](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Iss_after_installation_of_all_roll_out_solar_arrays.jpg/960px-Iss_after_installation_of_all_roll_out_solar_arrays.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Iss_after_installation_of_all_roll_out_solar_arrays.jpg "Rendering of ISS (as of 2023)")

Rendering of ISS (as of 2023)

Below is a diagram of major station components. The _Unity_ node joins directly to the _Destiny_ laboratory; for clarity, they are shown apart. Similar cases are also seen in other parts of the structure.

Key to box background colors:

*    Pressurised component, accessible by the crew without using spacesuits
*   [Docking/berthing port](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft "Docking and berthing of spacecraft"), pressurized when a visiting spacecraft is present
*   [Airlock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airlock "Airlock"), to move people or material between pressurized and unpressurized environment
*    Unpressurised station superstructure
*    Unpressurised component
*    Temporarily defunct or non-commissioned component
*    Former, no longer installed component
*    Future, not yet installed component

### Pressurised modules

#### _Zarya_

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg/250px-Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg)

_Zarya_ as seen by [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Endeavour_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour "Space Shuttle Endeavour") during [STS-88](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-88 "STS-88")

_Zarya_ ([Russian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_language "Russian language"): Заря, [lit.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literal_translation "Literal translation")'Sunrise'[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-108)), also known as the _Functional Cargo Block_ (Russian: Функционально-грузовой блок), was the inaugural component of the ISS. Launched in 1998, it initially served as the ISS's power source, storage, propulsion, and guidance system. As the station has grown, _Zarya'_ s role has transitioned primarily to storage, both internally and in its external fuel tanks.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA2-109)

A descendant of the [TKS spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TKS_(spacecraft) "TKS (spacecraft)") used in the [_Salyut_ programme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salyut_programme "Salyut programme"), _Zarya_ was built in Russia but is owned by the United States. Its name symbolizes the beginning of a new era of international space cooperation.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-russianspaceweb-enterprise-110)

#### _Unity_

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/ISS_Unity_module.jpg/250px-ISS_Unity_module.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_Unity_module.jpg)

_Unity_ as seen by [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Endeavour_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour "Space Shuttle Endeavour") during [STS-88](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-88 "STS-88")

_Unity_, also known as _Node 1_, is the inaugural U.S.-built component of the ISS.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nssdca-1998-069f-111)[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-112) Serving as the connection between the Russian and U.S. segments, this cylindrical module features six [Common Berthing Mechanism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_Berthing_Mechanism "Common Berthing Mechanism") locations ([forward](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forward_(aircraft) "Forward (aircraft)"), [aft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aft "Aft"), [port](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_(nautical) "Port (nautical)"), [starboard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starboard "Starboard"), [zenith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zenith "Zenith"), and [nadir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nadir "Nadir")) for attaching additional modules. Measuring 4.57 metres (15.0 ft) in diameter and 5.47 metres (17.9 ft) in length, _Unity_ was constructed of steel by [Boeing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing "Boeing") for NASA at the [Marshall Space Flight Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center "Marshall Space Flight Center") in [Huntsville, Alabama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama "Huntsville, Alabama"). It was the first of three connecting nodes – _Unity_, _Harmony_, and _Tranquility_ – that forms the structural backbone of the U.S. segment of the ISS.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-roy-20091020-113)

#### _Zvezda_

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Zvezda_%28tight_crop%29.jpg/250px-Zvezda_%28tight_crop%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Zvezda_(tight_crop).jpg)

_Zvezda_ as seen by [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Atlantis_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis "Space Shuttle Atlantis") during [STS-106](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-106 "STS-106")

_Zvezda_ (Russian: Звезда, lit.'star') launched in July 2000, is the core of the [Russian Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment "Russian Orbital Segment") of the ISS. Initially providing essential living quarters and [life support systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_support_system "Life support system"), it enabled the first continuous human presence aboard the station. While additional modules have expanded the ISS's capabilities, Zvezda remains the command and control center for the Russian segment and it is where crews gather during emergencies.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-114)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-115)[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-116)

A descendant of the Salyut programme's DOS spacecraft, Zvezda was built by [RKK Energia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RKK_Energia "RKK Energia") and launched atop a [Proton rocket](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton_(rocket_family) "Proton (rocket family)").[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ruspace-117)

#### _Destiny_

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/ISS_Destiny_Lab.jpg/250px-ISS_Destiny_Lab.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_Destiny_Lab.jpg)

The _Destiny_ module being installed on the ISS

The _Destiny_ laboratory is the primary research facility for U.S. experiments on the ISS. NASA's first permanent orbital research station since Skylab, the module was built by Boeing and launched aboard [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Atlantis_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis "Space Shuttle Atlantis") during [STS-98](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-98 "STS-98"). Attached to _Unity_ over a period of five days in February 2001, _Destiny_ has been a hub for scientific research ever since.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-boeing-118)[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-lab-119)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sts-98-120)

Within _Destiny_, astronauts conduct experiments in fields such as medicine, engineering, biotechnology, physics, materials science, and Earth science. Researchers worldwide benefit from these studies. The module also houses life support systems, including the [Oxygen Generating System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen_Generating_System "Oxygen Generating System").[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-121)

#### _Quest Joint Airlock_

[![Image 24](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Quest_airlock_exterior_-_STS-127.jpg/250px-Quest_airlock_exterior_-_STS-127.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Quest_airlock_exterior_-_STS-127.jpg)

_Quest_ Joint Airlock Module

The _Quest Joint Airlock_ enables [extravehicular activities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extravehicular_activity "Extravehicular activity") (EVAs) using either the U.S. [Extravehicular Mobility Unit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extravehicular_Mobility_Unit "Extravehicular Mobility Unit") (EMU) or the Russian [Orlan space suit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orlan_space_suit "Orlan space suit").[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-questairlock-122)

Before its installation, conducting EVAs from the ISS was challenging due to a variety of system and design differences. Only the Orlan suit could be used from the Transfer Chamber on the _Zvezda_ module (which was not a purpose-built airlock) and the EMU could only be used from the airlock on a visiting Space Shuttle, which could not accommodate the Orlan.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-usaf-2010-123)

Launched aboard [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Atlantis_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis "Space Shuttle Atlantis") during [STS-104](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-104 "STS-104") in July 2001 and attached to the Unity module, Quest is a 6.1-metre-long (20 ft), 4.0-metre-wide (13 ft) structure built by Boeing.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20210714-124) It houses the crew airlock for astronaut egress, an equipment airlock for suit storage, and has facilities to accommodate astronauts during their overnight pre-breathe procedures to prevent decompression sickness.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-usaf-2010-123)

The crew airlock, derived from the Space Shuttle, features essential equipment like lighting, handrails, and an Umbilical Interface Assembly (UIA) that provides life support and communication systems for up to two spacesuits simultaneously. These can be either two EMUs, two Orlan suits, or one of each design.

#### _Poisk_

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/STS-129_ISS-21_Poisk.jpg/250px-STS-129_ISS-21_Poisk.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-129_ISS-21_Poisk.jpg)

_Poisk_ with its own propulsion module (soon to be jettisoned)

_Poisk_ (Russian: По́иск, lit.'Search'), also known as the _Mini-Research Module 2_ (Russian: Малый исследовательский модуль 2), serves as both a secondary airlock on the Russian segment of the ISS and supports docking for Soyuz and Progress spacecraft, facilitates propellant transfers from the latter.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-125) Launched on 10 November 2009 attached to a modified [Progress spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_spacecraft "Progress spacecraft"), called [Progress M-MIM2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_M-MIM2 "Progress M-MIM2").[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-energia0828-126)[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20091110-127)

_Poisk_ provides facilities to maintain Orlan spacesuits and is equipped with two inward-opening hatches, a design change from _Mir_, which encountered a dangerous situation caused by an outward-opening hatch that opened too quickly because of a small amount of air pressure remaining in the airlock.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-russianspaceweb-mir-close-calls-128) Since the departure of [_Pirs_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pirs_(ISS_module) "Pirs (ISS module)") in 2021, it's become the sole airlock on the Russian segment.

#### _Harmony_

[![Image 26](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg/250px-Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg)

_Harmony_ (center) shown connected to _Columbus_, _Kibo_, and _Destiny_. The dark [PMA-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pressurized_Mating_Adapter "Pressurized Mating Adapter") faces the camera. The nadir and zenith locations are open.

_Harmony_, or _Node 2_, is the central connecting hub of the US segment of the ISS, linking the U.S., European, and Japanese laboratory modules. It's also been called the "utility hub" of the ISS as it provides essential power, data, and life support systems. The module also houses sleeping quarters for four crew members.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-129)

Launched on 23 October 2007 aboard [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Discovery_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery "Space Shuttle Discovery") on [STS-120](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-120 "STS-120"),[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-launch-130)[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-lat-131) Harmony was initially attached to the Unity[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-harmmate-132)[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-room-133) before being relocated to its permanent position at the front of the Destiny laboratory on 14 November 2007.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-pma3-134) This expansion added significant living space to the ISS, marking a key milestone in the construction of the U.S. segment.

#### _Tranquility_

[![Image 27](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg/250px-Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg)

_Tranquility_ in 2011

_Tranquility_, also known as _Node 3_, is a module of the ISS. It contains environmental control systems, [life support systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_support_system "Life support system"), a toilet, exercise equipment, and an observation [cupola](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupola_(ISS_module) "Cupola (ISS module)").

The European Space Agency and the [Italian Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_Space_Agency "Italian Space Agency") had _Tranquility_ manufactured by [Thales Alenia Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thales_Alenia_Space "Thales Alenia Space"). A ceremony on 20 November 2009 transferred ownership of the module to NASA.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-135) On 8 February 2010, NASA launched the module on the Space Shuttle's [STS-130](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-130 "STS-130") mission.

#### _Columbus_

[![Image 28](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Columbus_module_-_cropped.jpg/250px-Columbus_module_-_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Columbus_module_-_cropped.jpg)

The _Columbus_ module on the ISS

_Columbus_ is a science laboratory that is part of the ISS and is the largest single contribution to the station made by the European Space Agency.

Like the _Harmony_ and _Tranquility_ modules, the _Columbus_ laboratory was constructed in [Turin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turin "Turin"), Italy by [Thales Alenia Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thales_Alenia_Space "Thales Alenia Space"). The functional equipment and software of the lab was designed by [EADS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EADS "EADS") in [Bremen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bremen "Bremen"), Germany. It was also integrated in Bremen before being flown to the Kennedy Space Center in Florida in an [Airbus Beluga](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airbus_Beluga "Airbus Beluga") jet. It was launched aboard Space Shuttle _Atlantis_ on 7 February 2008, on flight [STS-122](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-122 "STS-122"). It is designed for ten years of operation. The module is controlled by the [Columbus Control Centre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbus_Control_Centre "Columbus Control Centre"), located at the [German Space Operations Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Space_Operations_Center "German Space Operations Center"), part of the [German Aerospace Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center "German Aerospace Center") in [Oberpfaffenhofen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oberpfaffenhofen "Oberpfaffenhofen") near [Munich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Munich "Munich"), Germany.

The European Space Agency has spent [€](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euro "Euro")1.4 billion (about [US$](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_dollar "United States dollar")1.6 billion) on building _Columbus_, including the experiments it carries and the ground control infrastructure necessary to operate them.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20080211-136)

#### _Kibō_

[![Image 29](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Japanese_Experiment_Module_exterior_-_cropped.jpg/250px-Japanese_Experiment_Module_exterior_-_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Japanese_Experiment_Module_exterior_-_cropped.jpg)

_Kibō_ with its exposed facility on the right

_Kibō_ ([Japanese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_language "Japanese language"): きぼう; lit.'hope'), also known as the _Japanese Experiment Module_, is Japan's research facility on the ISS. It is the largest single module on the ISS, consisting of a pressurized lab, an exposed facility for conducting experiments in the space environment, two storage compartments, and a robotic arm. Attached to the _Harmony_ module, _Kibō_ was assembled in space over three Space Shuttle missions: [STS-123](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-123 "STS-123"), [STS-124](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-124 "STS-124") and [STS-127](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-127 "STS-127").[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-japantimes-20090630-137)

#### _Cupola_

[![Image 30](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/STS130_cupola_view1.jpg/250px-STS130_cupola_view1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS130_cupola_view1.jpg)

The _Cupola_'s windows with shutters open

The _Cupola_ is an [ESA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ESA "ESA")-built observatory module of the ISS. Its name derives from the Italian word _\_cupola\__, which means "[dome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dome "Dome")". Its seven windows are used to conduct experiments, dockings and observations of Earth. It was launched aboard Space Shuttle mission STS-130 on 8 February 2010 and attached to the _Tranquility_ (Node 3) module. With the _Cupola_ attached, ISS assembly reached 85 per cent completion. The _Cupola_'s central window has a diameter of 80 cm (31 in).[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Cupola:_a_window_over_the_Earth-138)

#### _Rassvet_

[![Image 31](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/STS132_Mini-Research_Module-1.jpg/250px-STS132_Mini-Research_Module-1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS132_Mini-Research_Module-1.jpg)

_Rassvet_ module with MLM-outfitting equipment (consisting of experiment airlock, RTOd radiators, and ERA workpost) at KSC

_Rassvet_ (Russian: Рассвет, lit.'first light'), also known as the _Mini-Research Module 1_ (Russian: Малый исследовательский модуль 1) and formerly known as the _Docking Cargo Module_ is primarily used for cargo storage and as a docking port for visiting spacecraft on the Russian segment of the ISS. _Rassvet_ replaced the cancelled Docking and Storage Module and used a design largely based on the _[Mir Docking Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mir\_Docking\_Module "Mir Docking Module")_ built in 1995.

Rassvet was delivered in on 14 May 2010 [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Atlantis_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis "Space Shuttle Atlantis") on [STS-132](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-132 "STS-132") in exchange for the Russian Proton delivery of the US-funded _Zarya_ module in 1998.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20090409-139)_Rassvet_ was attached to _Zarya_ shortly thereafter.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-connect-140)

#### _Leonardo_

[![Image 32](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/STS-133_ISS-26_Permanent_Multipurpose_Module.jpg/250px-STS-133_ISS-26_Permanent_Multipurpose_Module.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-133_ISS-26_Permanent_Multipurpose_Module.jpg)

The _Leonardo_ module hours after berthing

The _Leonardo_ Permanent Multipurpose Module (PMM) was flown into space aboard the Space Shuttle _Discovery_ on [STS-133](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-133 "STS-133") on 24 February 2011 and installed on 1 March. _Leonardo_ is primarily used for storage of spares, supplies and waste on the ISS, which was until then stored in many different places within the space station. It is also the personal hygiene area for the astronauts who live in the [US Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment "US Orbital Segment"). The _Leonardo_ PMM was a [Multi-Purpose Logistics Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multi-Purpose_Logistics_Module "Multi-Purpose Logistics Module") (MPLM) before 2011, but was modified into its current configuration. It was formerly one of two MPLM used for bringing cargo to and from the ISS with the Space Shuttle. The module was named for Italian polymath [Leonardo da Vinci](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci "Leonardo da Vinci").

#### Bigelow Expandable Activity Module

[![Image 33](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/BEAM_module_expansion_series.jpg/250px-BEAM_module_expansion_series.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:BEAM_module_expansion_series.jpg)

Progression of the expansion of BEAM

The [Bigelow Expandable Activity Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bigelow_Expandable_Activity_Module "Bigelow Expandable Activity Module") (BEAM) is an experimental [expandable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inflatable_space_habitat "Inflatable space habitat")[space station module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#Pressurised_modules) developed by [Bigelow Aerospace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace "Bigelow Aerospace"), under contract to NASA, for testing as a temporary module on the International Space Station (ISS) from 2016 to at least 2020. It arrived at the ISS on 10 April 2016,[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-space20160410-141) was berthed to the station on 16 April at Tranquility Node 3, and was expanded and pressurized on 28 May 2016. In December 2021, Bigelow Aerospace conveyed ownership of the module to NASA, as a result of Bigelow's cessation of activity.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-142)

#### International Docking Adapters

The [International Docking Adapter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Docking_Adapter "International Docking Adapter") (IDA) is a [spacecraft docking system adapter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft#Adapters "Docking and berthing of spacecraft") developed to convert [APAS-95](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/APAS-95 "APAS-95") to the [NASA Docking System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Docking_System "NASA Docking System") (NDS). An IDA is placed on each of the ISS's two open [Pressurized Mating Adapters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pressurized_Mating_Adapters "Pressurized Mating Adapters") (PMAs), both of which are connected to the _Harmony_ module.

Two International Docking Adapters are currently installed aboard the Station. Originally, [IDA-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Docking_Adapter "International Docking Adapter") was planned to be installed on PMA-2, located at _Harmony_'s forward port, and [IDA-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Docking_Adapter#IDA-2 "International Docking Adapter") would be installed on PMA-3 at _Harmony_'s zenith.
