After IDA 1 was destroyed in [a launch incident](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-7 "SpaceX CRS-7"), [IDA-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Docking_Adapter#IDA-2 "International Docking Adapter") was installed on PMA-2 on 19 August 2016,[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20160819-143) while [IDA-3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Docking_Adapter#IDA-3 "International Docking Adapter") was later installed on PMA-3 on 21 August 2019.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-144)

#### Bishop Airlock Module

[![Image 34](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/ISS-64_NanoRacks_Bishop_airlock_after_installation.jpg/250px-ISS-64_NanoRacks_Bishop_airlock_after_installation.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-64_NanoRacks_Bishop_airlock_after_installation.jpg)

NanoRacks Bishop airlock module installed on the ISS

The NanoRacks Bishop Airlock Module is a [commercially](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_use_of_space "Commercial use of space") funded [airlock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airlock "Airlock") module launched to the ISS on [SpaceX CRS-21](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-21 "SpaceX CRS-21") on 6 December 2020.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-thales-milestone-145)[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20190802-146) The module was built by [NanoRacks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NanoRacks "NanoRacks"), [Thales Alenia Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thales_Alenia_Space "Thales Alenia Space"), and Boeing.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NanoRacks-147) It will be used to deploy [CubeSats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CubeSat "CubeSat"), [small satellites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Small_satellite "Small satellite"), and other external payloads for NASA, [CASIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CASIS "CASIS"), and other commercial and governmental customers.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_Announcement-148)

#### _Nauka_

[![Image 35](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Russian_Spacewalkers_dwarfed_by_the_Nauka_and_Prichal_modules_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Russian_Spacewalkers_dwarfed_by_the_Nauka_and_Prichal_modules_2_%28cropped%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Russian_Spacewalkers_dwarfed_by_the_Nauka_and_Prichal_modules_2_(cropped).jpg)

_Nauka_ and _Prichal_ docked to ISS

_Nauka_ (Russian: Наука, lit.'Science'), also known as the _Multipurpose Laboratory Module, Upgrade_ (Russian: Многоцелевой лабораторный модуль, усоверше́нствованный), is a Roscosmos-funded component of the ISS that was launched on 21 July 2021, 14:58 UTC. In the original ISS plans, _Nauka_ was to use the location of the [Docking and Stowage Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rassvet_(ISS_module)#Initial_planning "Rassvet (ISS module)") (DSM), but the DSM was later replaced by the _Rassvet_ module and moved to _Zarya_'s nadir port. _Nauka_ was successfully docked to _Zvezda_'s nadir port on 29 July 2021, 13:29 UTC, replacing the _Pirs_ module.

It had a temporary docking adapter on its nadir port for crewed and uncrewed missions until Prichal arrival, where just before its arrival it was removed by a departing Progress spacecraft.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-russianspaceweb-ms17-149)

#### _Prichal_

_Prichal_ (Russian: Причал, lit.'pier') is a 4-tonne (8,800 lb) spherical module that serves as a docking hub for the Russian segment of the ISS. Launched in November 2021, Prichal provides additional docking ports for Soyuz and Progress spacecraft, as well as potential future modules. _Prichal_ features six docking ports: forward, aft, port, starboard, zenith, and nadir. One of these ports, equipped with an active hybrid docking system, enabled it to dock with the Nauka module. The remaining five ports are passive hybrids, allowing for docking of Soyuz, Progress, and heavier modules, as well as future spacecraft with modified docking systems. As of 2024, the forward, aft, port and starboard docking ports remain covered. _Prichal_ was initially intended to be an element of the now canceled [Orbital Piloted Assembly and Experiment Complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_Piloted_Assembly_and_Experiment_Complex "Orbital Piloted Assembly and Experiment Complex").[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-rsw-um-150)[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:0-151)[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-energia-20110113-152)[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NSF20200819-153)

### Unpressurised elements

[![Image 36](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Truss_breakdown.png/250px-Truss_breakdown.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Truss_breakdown.png)

ISS Truss Components breakdown showing Trusses and all ORUs in situ

[![Image 37](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg/250px-STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg)

The ISS has a large number of external components that do not require pressurisation. The largest of these is the [Integrated Truss Structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure "Integrated Truss Structure") (ITS), to which the station's main [solar arrays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photovoltaic_system "Photovoltaic system") and [thermal radiators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/External_Active_Thermal_Control_System "External Active Thermal Control System") are mounted.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Arrays-154) The ITS consists of ten separate segments forming a structure 108.5 metres (356 ft) long.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-OnOrbit-8)

The station was intended to have several smaller external components, such as six robotic arms, three [External Stowage Platforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/External_Stowage_Platform "External Stowage Platform") (ESPs) and four [ExPRESS Logistics Carriers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExPRESS_Logistics_Carrier "ExPRESS Logistics Carrier") (ELCs).[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Manifest-155)[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-factsheet-expressracks-156) While these platforms allow experiments (including [MISSE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materials_International_Space_Station_Experiment "Materials International Space Station Experiment"), the STP-H3 and the [Robotic Refueling Mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robotic_Refueling_Mission "Robotic Refueling Mission")) to be deployed and conducted in the vacuum of space by providing electricity and processing experimental data locally, their primary function is to store spare [Orbital Replacement Units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_replacement_unit "Orbital replacement unit") (ORUs). ORUs are parts that can be replaced when they fail or pass their design life, including pumps, storage tanks, antennas, and battery units. Such units are replaced either by astronauts during EVA or by robotic arms.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20111223-157) Several shuttle missions were dedicated to the delivery of ORUs, including [STS-129](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-129 "STS-129"),[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-EVA129-158) STS-133[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sts-133-press-kit-159) and STS-134.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sts-134-press-kit-160) As of January 2011, only one other mode of transportation of ORUs had been used– the Japanese cargo vessel [HTV-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle "H-II Transfer Vehicle")– which delivered an FHRC and CTC-2 via its Exposed Pallet (EP).[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-HTV2-161)[_[needs update](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual\_of\_Style/Dates\_and\_numbers#Chronological\_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_]

There are also smaller exposure facilities mounted directly to laboratory modules; the _Kibō_[Exposed Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kibo_(ISS_module) "Kibo (ISS module)") serves as an external "[porch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Porch "Porch")" for the _Kibō_ complex,[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-162) and a facility on the European _Columbus_ laboratory provides power and data connections for experiments such as the [European Technology Exposure Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Technology_Exposure_Facility "European Technology Exposure Facility")[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA-163)[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ESA-164) and the [Atomic Clock Ensemble in Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_Clock_Ensemble_in_Space "Atomic Clock Ensemble in Space").[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-165) A [remote sensing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Remote_sensing "Remote sensing") instrument, [SAGE III-ISS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SAGE_III_on_ISS "SAGE III on ISS"), was delivered to the station in February 2017 aboard [CRS-10](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-10 "SpaceX CRS-10"),[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf20170310-166) and the [NICER](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_Star_Interior_Composition_Explorer "Neutron Star Interior Composition Explorer") experiment was delivered aboard [CRS-11](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-11 "SpaceX CRS-11") in June 2017.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf20170603-167) The largest scientific payload externally mounted to the ISS is the [Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer "Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer") (AMS), a particle physics experiment launched on STS-134 in May 2011, and mounted externally on the ITS. The AMS measures cosmic rays to look for evidence of dark matter and antimatter.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ams-cern-168)[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf20130404-169)

The commercial _[Bartolomeo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbus\_%E2%80%93\_External\_Payload\_Facility#Bartolomeo "Columbus – External Payload Facility")_ External Payload Hosting Platform, manufactured by Airbus, was launched on 6 March 2020 aboard [CRS-20](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-20 "SpaceX CRS-20") and attached to the European _Columbus_ module. It will provide an additional 12 external payload slots, supplementing the eight on the [ExPRESS Logistics Carriers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExPRESS_Logistics_Carrier "ExPRESS Logistics Carrier"), ten on _Kibō_, and four on _Columbus_. The system is designed to be robotically serviced and will require no astronaut intervention. It is named after Christopher Columbus's younger brother.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacedaily-20180209-170)[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-aerospacetech-20180208-171)[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-eoportal-iss-bartolomeo-172)

#### MLM outfittings

[![Image 38](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/MRM1_Rassvet.png/250px-MRM1_Rassvet.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:MRM1_Rassvet.png)

MLM outfittings on Rassvet

[![Image 39](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ISS-65_Nauka_and_Soyuz_MS-18_docked_to_the_International_Space_Station_%281%29.jpg/250px-ISS-65_Nauka_and_Soyuz_MS-18_docked_to_the_International_Space_Station_%281%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-65_Nauka_and_Soyuz_MS-18_docked_to_the_International_Space_Station_(1).jpg)

A wide-angle view of the new module (behind _Rassvet_) attached to the [ROS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment "Russian Orbital Segment") as seen from the cupola

In May 2010, equipment for _Nauka_ was launched on STS-132 (as part of an agreement with NASA) and delivered by Space Shuttle _Atlantis_. Weighing 1.4 metric tons, the equipment was attached to the outside of _Rassvet_ (MRM-1). It included a spare elbow joint for the [European Robotic Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Robotic_Arm "European Robotic Arm") (ERA) (which was launched with _Nauka_) and an ERA-portable workpost used during EVAs, as well as RTOd add-on heat radiator and internal hardware alongside the pressurized experiment airlock.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Roscosmos1-173)

The RTOd radiator adds additional cooling capability to _Nauka_, which enables the module to host more scientific experiments.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Roscosmos1-173)

The ERA was used to remove the RTOd radiator from _Rassvet_ and transferred over to _Nauka_ during VKD-56 spacewalk. Later it was activated and fully deployed on VKD-58 spacewalk.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-garcia-20230512-174) This process took several months. A portable work platform was also transferred over in August 2023 during VKD-60 spacewalk, which can attach to the end of the ERA to allow cosmonauts to "ride" on the end of the arm during spacewalks.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa-arm-brochure-175)[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20230809-176) However, even after several months of outfitting EVAs and RTOd heat radiator installation, six months later, the RTOd radiator malfunctioned before active use of Nauka (the purpose of RTOd installation is to radiate heat from Nauka experiments). The malfunction, a leak, rendered the RTOd radiator unusable for Nauka. This is the third ISS radiator leak after [Soyuz MS-22](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_MS-22 "Soyuz MS-22") and [Progress MS-21](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_MS-21 "Progress MS-21") radiator leaks. If a spare RTOd is not available, Nauka experiments will have to rely on Nauka's main launch radiator and the module could never be used to its full capacity.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20231009-177)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-178)

Another MLM outfitting is a 4 segment external payload interface called means of attachment of large payloads (Sredstva Krepleniya Krupnogabaritnykh Obyektov, SKKO).[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-179) Delivered in two parts to Nauka by [Progress MS-18](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_MS-18 "Progress MS-18") (LCCS part) and [Progress MS-21](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_MS-21 "Progress MS-21") (SCCCS part) as part of the module activation outfitting process.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-180)[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-181)[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-182)[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-russianspaceweb-fgb2-mlm-183) It was taken outside and installed on the ERA aft facing base point on Nauka during the VKD-55 spacewalk.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-garcia-20221116-184)[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-lavelle-20221117-185)[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-garcia-20221117-186)[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacecom-20221117-187)

#### Robotic arms and cargo cranes

[![Image 40](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Iss017e011097.jpg/250px-Iss017e011097.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Iss017e011097.jpg)

[![Image 41](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg/250px-Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg)

[Dextre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dextre "Dextre"), like many of the station's experiments and robotic arms, can be operated from Earth, allowing tasks to be performed while the crew sleeps.

The Integrated Truss Structure (ITS) serves as a base for the station's primary remote manipulator system, the [Mobile Servicing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_Servicing_System "Mobile Servicing System") (MSS), which is composed of three main components:

*   [Canadarm2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadarm2 "Canadarm2"), the largest robotic arm on the ISS, has a mass of 1,800 kilograms (4,000 lb) and is used to: dock and manipulate spacecraft and modules on the USOS; hold crew members and equipment in place during EVAs; and move Dextre to perform tasks.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-188)
*   [Dextre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dextre "Dextre") is a 1,560 kg (3,440 lb) robotic manipulator that has two arms and a rotating torso, with power tools, lights, and video for replacing [orbital replacement units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_replacement_unit "Orbital replacement unit") (ORUs) and performing other tasks requiring fine control.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-csa-dextre-189)
*   The [Mobile Base System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_Base_System "Mobile Base System") (MBS) is a platform that rides on rails along the length of the station's main truss, which serves as a mobile base for Canadarm2 and Dextre, allowing the robotic arms to reach all parts of the USOS.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-csa-mbs-190)

A [grapple fixture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grapple_Fixture "Grapple Fixture") was added to _Zarya_ on STS-134 to enable Canadarm2 to inchworm itself onto the ROS.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sts-134-press-kit-160) Also installed during STS-134 was the 15 m (50 ft) [Orbiter Boom Sensor System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiter_Boom_Sensor_System "Orbiter Boom Sensor System") (OBSS), which had been used to inspect heat shield tiles on Space Shuttle missions and which can be used on the station to increase the reach of the MSS.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sts-134-press-kit-160) Staff on Earth or the ISS can operate the MSS components using remote control, performing work outside the station without the need for space walks.

Japan's [Remote Manipulator System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_Remote_Manipulator_System "Japanese Remote Manipulator System"), which services the _Kibō_ Exposed Facility,[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-191) was launched on [STS-124](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-124 "STS-124") and is attached to the _Kibō_ Pressurised Module.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-192) The arm is similar to the Space Shuttle arm as it is permanently attached at one end and has a latching end effector for standard grapple fixtures at the other.

The [European Robotic Arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Robotic_Arm "European Robotic Arm"), which will service the ROS, was launched alongside the [_Nauka_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module) "Nauka (ISS module)") module.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tass-20200402-193) The ROS does not require spacecraft or modules to be manipulated, as all spacecraft and modules dock automatically and may be discarded the same way. Crew use the two _[Strela](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strela\_(crane) "Strela (crane)")_ ([Russian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_language "Russian language"): Стрела́, [lit.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literal_translation "Literal translation")'Arrow') cargo cranes during EVAs for moving crew and equipment around the ROS. Each Strela crane has a mass of 45 kg (99 lb).

### Former module

[![Image 42](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sts110-363-001.jpg/250px-Sts110-363-001.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sts110-363-001.jpg)

The _Pirs_ module attached to the ISS

[![Image 43](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/ISS-65_Pirs_docking_compartment_separates_from_the_Space_Station.jpg/250px-ISS-65_Pirs_docking_compartment_separates_from_the_Space_Station.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-65_Pirs_docking_compartment_separates_from_the_Space_Station.jpg)

ISS-65 Pirs docking compartment separates from the International Space Station.

#### _Pirs_

Pirs (Russian: Пирс, lit. 'Pier') was launched on 14 September 2001, as ISS Assembly Mission 4R, on a Russian Soyuz-U rocket, using a modified [Progress spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_spacecraft "Progress spacecraft"), [Progress M-SO1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_M-SO1 "Progress M-SO1"), as an upper stage. Pirs was undocked by [Progress MS-16](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_MS-16 "Progress MS-16") on 26 July 2021, 10:56 UTC, and deorbited on the same day at 14:51 UTC to make room for the [_Nauka_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module) "Nauka (ISS module)") module to be attached to the space station. Prior to its departure, Pirs served as the primary Russian airlock on the station, being used to store and refurbish the Russian Orlan spacesuits.

### Planned components

#### Axiom segment

[![Image 44](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Axiom_modules_connected_to_ISS.jpg/250px-Axiom_modules_connected_to_ISS.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Axiom_modules_connected_to_ISS.jpg)

Early rendering of the [Axiom Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Orbital_Segment "Axiom Orbital Segment"), made prior to assembly plan changes

In January 2020, NASA awarded [Axiom Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Space "Axiom Space") a contract to build a commercial module for the ISS. The contract is under the [NextSTEP2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Next_Space_Technologies_for_Exploration_Partnerships "Next Space Technologies for Exploration Partnerships") program. NASA negotiated with Axiom on a firm [fixed-price contract](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fixed-price_contract "Fixed-price contract") basis to build and deliver the module, which will attach to the forward port of the space station's _Harmony (Node 2)_ module. Although NASA only commissioned one module, Axiom planned to build an entire segment consisting of five modules, including a node module, an orbital research and manufacturing facility, a crew habitat, and a "large-windowed Earth observatory". The Axiom segment was expected to greatly increase the capabilities and value of the space station, allowing for larger crews and private spaceflight by other organisations. Axiom planned to convert the segment into a stand-alone space station once the ISS is decommissioned, with the intention that this would act as a successor to the ISS.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20200128-194)[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-techcrunch-20200127-195)[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-geekwire-20200127-196)[Canadarm2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadarm2 "Canadarm2") is planned to continue its operations on Axiom Station after the retirement of ISS in 2030.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-axiom-assembly-197) In December 2024, Axiom Space revised their station assembly plans to require only one module to dock with the ISS before assembling Axiom Station in an independent orbit.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sn-20241218-198)

As of December 2024, Axiom Space expects to launch one module, the Payload Power Thermal Module (PPTM), to the ISS no earlier than 2027.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sn-20241218-198) PPTM is expected to remain at the ISS until the launch of Axiom's Habitat One (Hab-1) module about one year later, after which it will detach from the ISS to join with Hab-1.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sn-20241218-198)

#### US Deorbit Vehicle

The [US Deorbit Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Deorbit_Vehicle "US Deorbit Vehicle") (USDV) is a NASA-provided spacecraft intended to perform a controlled de-orbit and demise of the station after the end of its operational life in 2030. In June 2024, NASA awarded [SpaceX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX "SpaceX") a contract to build the Deorbit Vehicle.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:2-199) NASA plans to de-orbit ISS as soon as they have the "minimum capability" in orbit: "the USDV and at least one commercial station."[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-200)

### Cancelled components

[![Image 45](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/ISS_Habitation_module.jpg/250px-ISS_Habitation_module.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_Habitation_module.jpg)

The cancelled Habitation module under construction at Michoud in 1997

[![Image 46](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Nautilus-X_demonstrator_docked_to_the_ISS_%28side%29.webp/250px-Nautilus-X_demonstrator_docked_to_the_ISS_%28side%29.webp.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nautilus-X_demonstrator_docked_to_the_ISS_(side).webp)

Rendering of the Nautilus-X Centrifuge Demonstrator docked to the ISS (side)

Several modules developed or planned for the station were cancelled over the course of the ISS programme. Reasons include budgetary constraints, the modules becoming unnecessary, and station redesigns after the 2003 [_Columbia_ disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster "Space Shuttle Columbia disaster"). The US [Centrifuge Accommodations Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrifuge_Accommodations_Module "Centrifuge Accommodations Module") would have hosted science experiments in varying levels of [artificial gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_gravity "Artificial gravity").[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-201) The US [Habitation Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitation_Module "Habitation Module") would have served as the station's living quarters. Instead, the living quarters are now spread throughout the station.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacecom-20060214-202) The US [Interim Control Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interim_Control_Module "Interim Control Module") and [ISS Propulsion Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISS_Propulsion_Module "ISS Propulsion Module") would have replaced the functions of _Zvezda_ in case of a launch failure.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-navy-code8200-icm-203) Two [Russian Research Modules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Research_Module "Russian Research Module") were planned for scientific research.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-boeing-russian-modules-204) They would have docked to a Russian [Universal Docking Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universal_Docking_Module "Universal Docking Module").[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-russianspaceweb-iss-segment-205) The Russian [Science Power Platform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_Power_Platform "Science Power Platform") would have supplied power to the Russian Orbital Segment independent of the ITS solar arrays.

#### Science Power Modules 1 and 2 (Repurposed Components)

**Science Power Module 1** (**SPM-1**, also known as **NEM-1**) and **Science Power Module 2** (**SPM-2**, also known as **NEM-2**) are modules that were originally planned to arrive at the ISS no earlier than 2024, and dock to the _Prichal_ module, which is docked to the _Nauka_ module.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NSF20200819-153)[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-rsw-2024-206) In April 2021, Roscosmos announced that NEM-1 would be repurposed to function as the core module of the proposed [Russian Orbital Service Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Service_Station "Russian Orbital Service Station") (ROSS), launching no earlier than 2027[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tass-20230124-207) and docking to the free-flying _Nauka_ module.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tass-20210719-208)[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-russianspaceweb-ros-209) NEM-2 may be converted into another core "base" module, which would be launched in 2028.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-roscosmos-20210424-210)

#### Xbase

Main article: [B330](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B330 "B330")

Designed by [Bigelow Aerospace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace "Bigelow Aerospace"). In August 2016, Bigelow negotiated an agreement with NASA to develop a full-size ground prototype Deep Space Habitation based on the B330 under the second phase of Next Space Technologies for Exploration Partnerships. The module was called the Expandable Bigelow Advanced Station Enhancement (XBASE), as Bigelow hoped to test the module by attaching it to the International Space Station. However, in March 2020, Bigelow laid off all 88 of its employees, and as of February 2024 the company remains dormant and is considered defunct,[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-211)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-212) making it appear unlikely that the XBASE module will ever be launched.

#### Nautilus-X Centrifuge Demonstration

A proposal was put forward in 2011 for a first in-space demonstration of a sufficiently scaled centrifuge for artificial partial-g gravity effects. It was designed to become a sleep module for the ISS crew. The project was cancelled in favour of other projects due to budget constraints.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-m966-213)

Onboard systems
---------------

### Life support

The critical systems are the atmosphere control system, the water supply system, the food supply facilities, the sanitation and hygiene equipment, and fire detection and suppression equipment. The Russian Orbital Segment's life support systems are contained in the _Zvezda_ service module. Some of these systems are supplemented by equipment in the USOS. The _Nauka_ laboratory has a complete set of life support systems.

#### Atmospheric control systems

[![Image 47: A flowchart diagram showing the components of the ISS life support system.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SpaceStationCycle.svg/500px-SpaceStationCycle.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SpaceStationCycle.svg)

The interactions between the components of the ISS Environmental Control and Life Support System (ECLSS)

The atmosphere on board the ISS is similar to [that of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth").[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-214) Normal air pressure on the ISS is 101.3 kPa (14.69 psi);[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-215) the same as at sea level on Earth. An Earth-like atmosphere offers benefits for crew comfort, and is much safer than a pure oxygen atmosphere, because of the increased risk of a fire such as that responsible for the deaths of the [Apollo 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_1 "Apollo 1") crew.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-apollo-204-report-216)[_[better source needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS "Wikipedia:NOTRS")_] Earth-like atmospheric conditions have been maintained on all Russian and Soviet spacecraft.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacemed-217)

The _[Elektron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elektron\_(ISS) "Elektron (ISS)")_ system aboard _Zvezda_ and a similar system in _Destiny_ generate oxygen aboard the station.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-OGS-218) The crew has a backup option in the form of bottled oxygen and [Solid Fuel Oxygen Generation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vika_oxygen_generator "Vika oxygen generator") (SFOG) canisters, a [chemical oxygen generator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator "Chemical oxygen generator") system.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-breath_easy-219) Carbon dioxide is removed from the air by the [Vozdukh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISS_ECLSS#Vozdukh "ISS ECLSS") system in _Zvezda_. Other by-products of human metabolism, such as methane from the intestines and ammonia from sweat, are removed by [activated charcoal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Activated_carbon "Activated carbon") filters.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-breath_easy-219)

Part of the ROS atmosphere control system is the oxygen supply. Triple-redundancy is provided by the Elektron unit, solid fuel generators, and stored oxygen. The primary supply of oxygen is the Elektron unit which produces O 2 and H 2 by [electrolysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrolysis "Electrolysis") of water and vents H 2 overboard. The 1 kW (1.3 hp) system uses approximately one litre of water per crew member per day. This water is either brought from Earth or recycled from other systems. _Mir_ was the first spacecraft to use recycled water for oxygen production. The secondary oxygen supply is provided by burning oxygen-producing [Vika](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vika_oxygen_generator "Vika oxygen generator") cartridges (see also [ISS ECLSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISS_ECLSS "ISS ECLSS")). Each 'candle' takes 5–20 minutes to decompose at 450–500°C (842–932°F), producing 600 litres (130 imp gal; 160 US gal) of O 2. This unit is manually operated.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-220)

The US Orbital Segment (USOS) has redundant supplies of oxygen, from a pressurised storage tank on the _Quest_ airlock module delivered in 2001, supplemented ten years later by ESA-built Advanced Closed-Loop System (ACLS) in the _Tranquility_ module (Node 3), which produces O 2 by electrolysis.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20001113-221) Hydrogen produced is combined with carbon dioxide from the cabin atmosphere and converted to water and methane.

### Power and thermal control

[![Image 48](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ROSSA.jpg/120px-ROSSA.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ROSSA.jpg)

Russian solar arrays, backlit by sunset

[![Image 49](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/P4_deployed.jpg/120px-P4_deployed.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:P4_deployed.jpg)

One of the eight truss mounted pairs of USOS solar arrays

[![Image 50](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/ISS_new_iROSA_deployed.jpg/250px-ISS_new_iROSA_deployed.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_new_iROSA_deployed.jpg)

ISS new roll out solar array as seen from a zoom camera on the P6 Truss

Double-sided [solar arrays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_panel "Solar panel") provide [electrical power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_power "Electric power") to the ISS. These bifacial cells collect direct sunlight on one side and light [reflected off](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo "Albedo") from the Earth on the other, and are more efficient and operate at a lower temperature than single-sided cells commonly used on Earth.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-andreas-2005-222)

The Russian segment of the station, like most spacecraft, uses 28[V](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volt "Volt")[low voltage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extra-low_voltage "Extra-low voltage")[DC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Direct_current "Direct current") from two rotating solar arrays mounted on _Zvezda_. The USOS uses 130–180 V DC from the USOS PV array. Power is stabilised and distributed at 160 V DC and converted to the user-required 124 V DC. The [higher distribution voltage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_voltage "High voltage") allows smaller, lighter conductors, at the expense of crew safety. The two station segments share power with converters.

The USOS solar arrays are arranged as four wing pairs, for a total production of 75 to 90 kilowatts.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISS_stats-3) These arrays normally track the Sun to maximise power generation. Each array is about 375 m 2 (4,036 sq ft) in area and 58 m (190 ft) long. In the complete configuration, the solar arrays track the Sun by rotating the _alpha [gimbal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gimbal "Gimbal")_ once per orbit; the _beta gimbal_ follows slower changes in the angle of the Sun to the orbital plane. The [Night Glider mode](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Night_Glider_mode "Night Glider mode") aligns the solar arrays parallel to the ground at night to reduce the significant aerodynamic drag at the station's relatively low orbital altitude.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-223)

The station originally used rechargeable [nickel–hydrogen batteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93hydrogen_battery "Nickel–hydrogen battery") (NiH 2) for continuous power during the 45 minutes of every 90-minute orbit that it is eclipsed by the Earth. The batteries are recharged on the day side of the orbit. They had a 6.5-year lifetime (over 37,000 charge/discharge cycles) and were regularly replaced over the anticipated 20-year life of the station.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-224) Starting in 2016, the nickel–hydrogen batteries were replaced by [lithium-ion batteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery "Lithium-ion battery"), which are expected to last until the end of the ISS program.[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20161213-225)

The station's large solar panels generate a high potential voltage difference between the station and the ionosphere. This could cause arcing through insulating surfaces and sputtering of conductive surfaces as ions are accelerated by the spacecraft plasma sheath. To mitigate this, [plasma contactor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plasma_contactor "Plasma contactor") units create current paths between the station and the ambient space plasma.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-226)

[![Image 51](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/EATCS.png/550px-EATCS.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:EATCS.png)

ISS External Active Thermal Control System (EATCS) diagram

The station's systems and experiments consume a large amount of electrical power, almost all of which is converted to heat. To keep the internal temperature within workable limits, a passive thermal control system (PTCS) is made of external surface materials, insulation such as MLI, and heat pipes. If the PTCS cannot keep up with the heat load, an External Active Thermal Control System (EATCS) maintains the temperature. The EATCS consists of an internal, non-toxic, water coolant loop used to cool and dehumidify the atmosphere, which transfers collected heat into an external liquid [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia") loop. From the heat exchangers, ammonia is pumped into external radiators that emit heat as infrared radiation, then the ammonia is cycled back to the station.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-stayingcool-227) The EATCS provides cooling for all the US pressurised modules, including _Kibō_ and _Columbus_, as well as the main power distribution electronics of the S0, S1 and P1 trusses. It can reject up to 70 kW. This is much more than the 14 kW of the Early External Active Thermal Control System (EEATCS) via the Early Ammonia Servicer (EAS), which was launched on [STS-105](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-105 "STS-105") and installed onto the P6 Truss.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-acts-overview-228)

### Communications and computers

The ISS relies on various radio communication systems to provide [telemetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telemetry "Telemetry") and scientific data links between the station and [mission control centres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mission_control_center "Mission control center"). Radio links are also used during [rendezvous and docking procedures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_rendezvous "Space rendezvous") and for audio and video communication between crew members, flight controllers and family members. As a result, the ISS is equipped with internal and external communication systems used for different purposes.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-BoeingComm-229)

The Russian Orbital Segment primarily uses the _[Lira](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lira\_(ISS) "Lira (ISS)")_ antenna mounted on _Zvezda_ for direct ground communication.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSRG-63)[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-230) It also had the capability to utilize the _[Luch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luch\_(satellite) "Luch (satellite)")_ data relay satellite system,[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSRG-63) which was in a state of disrepair when the station was built,[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSRG-63)[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-SSSM-231)[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Harvey-232) but was restored to operational status in 2011 and 2012 with the launch of Luch-5A and Luch-5B.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-233) Additionally, the [Voskhod-M](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voskhod-M "Voskhod-M") system provides internal telephone communications and VHF radio links to ground control.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-234)

The [US Orbital Segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment "US Orbital Segment") (USOS) makes use of two separate radio links: [S band](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S_band "S band") (audio, telemetry, commanding – located on the P1/S1 truss) and [K u band](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ku_band "Ku band") (audio, video and data – located on the [Z1 truss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Z1_truss "Z1 truss")) systems. These transmissions are routed via the United States [Tracking and Data Relay Satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tracking_and_Data_Relay_Satellite "Tracking and Data Relay Satellite") System (TDRSS) in [geostationary orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geostationary_orbit "Geostationary orbit"), allowing for almost continuous real-time communications with [Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center "Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center") (MCC-H) in [Houston](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houston "Houston"), Texas.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSRG-63)[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSBook-235)[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-BoeingComm-229) Data channels for the Canadarm2, European _Columbus_ laboratory and Japanese _Kibō_ modules were originally also routed via the S band and K u band systems, with the [European Data Relay System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Data_Relay_System "European Data Relay System") and a similar Japanese system intended to eventually complement the TDRSS in this role.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSBook-235)[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-JAXA-MOU-236)

[UHF radio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultra_high_frequency "Ultra high frequency") is used by astronauts and cosmonauts conducting EVAs and other spacecraft that dock to or undock from the station.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSRG-63) Automated spacecraft are fitted with their own communications equipment; the ATV used a [laser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laser "Laser") attached to the spacecraft and the Proximity Communications Equipment attached to _Zvezda_ to accurately dock with the station.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spaceref-20050228-237)[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20091110-238)

[![Image 52](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/ISS-38_EVA-1_Laptops.jpg/250px-ISS-38_EVA-1_Laptops.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-38_EVA-1_Laptops.jpg)

An array of laptops in the US lab

[![Image 53](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg/250px-STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg)

Laptop computers surround the Canadarm2 console.

[![Image 54](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/ISS_laptop_hard_drive_failure_error_message.jpg/250px-ISS_laptop_hard_drive_failure_error_message.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_laptop_hard_drive_failure_error_message.jpg)

An error message displays a problem with a hard drive on a laptop aboard the ISS.

The US Orbital Segment of the ISS is equipped with approximately 100 [commercial off-the-shelf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_off-the-shelf "Commercial off-the-shelf") laptops running Windows or Linux.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-techrepublic-20160523-239) These devices are modified to use the station's 28V DC power system and with additional ventilation since heat generated by the devices can stagnate in the weightless environment. NASA prefers to keep a high commonality between laptops and spare parts are kept on the station so astronauts can repair laptops when needed.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa-20130813-240)

The laptops are divided into two groups: the Portable Computer System (PCS) and Station Support Computers (SSC).

PCS laptops run Linux and are used for connecting to the station's primary Command & Control computer (C&C MDM), which runs on [Debian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Debian "Debian") Linux,[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-register-20130510-241) a switch made from Windows in 2013 for reliability and flexibility.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-242) The primary computer supervises the critical systems that keep the station in orbit and supporting life.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-techrepublic-20160523-239) Since the primary computer has no display or keyboards, astronauts use a PCS laptop to connect as remote terminals via a USB to [1553](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIL-STD-1553 "MIL-STD-1553") adapter.[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20190430-243) The primary computer experienced failures in 2001,[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-roundup20010601-244) 2007,[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-reuters20070613-245) and 2017. The 2017 failure required a spacewalk to replace external components.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-huffpost20170522-246)

SSC laptops are used for everything else on the station, including reviewing procedures, managing scientific experiments, communicating over e-mail or video chat, and for entertainment during downtime.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-techrepublic-20160523-239) SSC laptops connect to the station's [wireless LAN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wireless_LAN "Wireless LAN") via [Wi-Fi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wi-Fi "Wi-Fi"), which connects to the ground via the K u band. While originally this provided speeds of 10[Mbit/s](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megabit_per_second "Megabit per second") download and 3 Mbit/s upload from the station,[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tested20121019-247) NASA upgraded the system in 2019 and increased the speeds to 600 Mbit/s.[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-universetoday-20190825-248) ISS crew members have access to the [internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet "Internet").[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Kuksov-249)[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ScienceAlert-250)

Operations
----------

### Expeditions

[![Image 55](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sts088-703-019e.jpg/250px-Sts088-703-019e.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Sts088-703-019e.jpg)

_Zarya_ and _Unity_ were entered for the first time on 10 December 1998.

[![Image 56](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg/250px-Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg)

Soyuz TM-31 being prepared to bring the first resident crew to the station in October 2000

Each permanent crew is given an expedition number. Expeditions run up to six months, from launch until undocking, an 'increment' covers the same time period, but includes cargo spacecraft and all activities. Expeditions 1 to 6 consisted of three-person crews. After the destruction of NASA's Space Shuttle _Columbia_, Expeditions 7 to 12 were reduced to two-person "caretaker" crews who could maintain the station, because a larger crew could not be fully resupplied by the small Russian Progress cargo spacecraft.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-251) After the Shuttle fleet returned to flight, three person crews also returned to the ISS beginning with Expedition 13. As the Shuttle flights expanded the station, crew sizes also expanded, eventually reaching six around 2010.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSEx-252)[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-current-253) With the arrival of crew on larger US [commercial spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Development_of_the_Commercial_Crew_Program "Development of the Commercial Crew Program") beginning in 2020,[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-bbc-20200119-254) crew size has been increased to seven, the number for which ISS was originally designed.[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-255)[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-airandspace-201104-256)

[Oleg Kononenko](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oleg_Kononenko "Oleg Kononenko") of Roscosmos holds the record for the longest time spent in space and at the ISS, accumulating nearly 1,111 days in space over the course of five long-duration missions on the ISS ([Expedition 17](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_17 "Expedition 17"), [30](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_30 "Expedition 30")/[31](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_31 "Expedition 31"), [44](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_44 "Expedition 44")/[45](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_45 "Expedition 45"), [57](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_57 "Expedition 57")/[58](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_58 "Expedition 58")/[59](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_59 "Expedition 59") and [69](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_69 "Expedition 69")/[70](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_70 "Expedition 70")/[71](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_71 "Expedition 71")). He also served as commander three times (Expedition 31, 58/59 and 70/71).[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-257)

[Peggy Whitson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peggy_Whitson "Peggy Whitson") of NASA and [Axiom Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Space "Axiom Space") has spent the most time in space of any American, accumulating over 675 days in space during her time on Expeditions [5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_5 "Expedition 5"), [16](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_16 "Expedition 16"), and [50](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_50 "Expedition 50")/[51](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_51 "Expedition 51")/[52](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_52 "Expedition 52") and Axiom Missions [2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Mission_2 "Axiom Mission 2") and [4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Mission_4 "Axiom Mission 4").[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-258)[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ax2commander-259)

### Private flights

Travellers who pay for their own passage into space are termed [spaceflight participants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaceflight_participant "Spaceflight participant") by Roscosmos and NASA, and are sometimes referred to as "space tourists", a term they generally dislike.[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-267) As of June 2023, thirteen space tourists have visited the ISS; nine were transported to the ISS on Russian Soyuz spacecraft, and four were transported on American [SpaceX Dragon 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon_2 "SpaceX Dragon 2") spacecraft. For one-tourist missions, when professional crews change over in numbers not divisible by the three seats in a Soyuz, and a short-stay crewmember is not sent, the spare seat is sold by MirCorp through Space Adventures. Space tourism was halted in 2011 when the Space Shuttle was retired and the station's crew size was reduced to six, as the partners relied on Russian transport seats for access to the station. Soyuz flight schedules increased after 2013, allowing five Soyuz flights (15 seats) with only two expeditions (12 seats) required.[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20110112-268) The remaining seats were to be sold for around US$40 million each to members of the public who could pass a medical exam. ESA and NASA criticised private spaceflight at the beginning of the ISS, and NASA initially resisted training [Dennis Tito](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dennis_Tito "Dennis Tito"), the first person to pay for his own passage to the ISS.[[e]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-269)

[Anousheh Ansari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anousheh_Ansari "Anousheh Ansari") became the first self-funded woman to fly to the ISS as well as the first Iranian in space. Officials reported that her education and experience made her much more than a tourist, and her performance in training had been "excellent."[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-rfe-20060915-270) She did Russian and European studies involving medicine and microbiology during her 10-day stay. The 2009 documentary _[Space Tourists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space\_Tourists "Space Tourists")_ follows her journey to the station, where she fulfilled "an age-old dream of man: to leave our planet as a 'normal person' and travel into outer space."[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-space-tourists-film-271)

In 2008, spaceflight participant [Richard Garriott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Garriott "Richard Garriott") placed a [geocache](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geocache "Geocache") aboard the ISS during his flight.[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-272) This is the first non-terrestrial geocache in history.[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-geekwire-20110829-273) At the same time, the [Immortality Drive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immortality_Drive "Immortality Drive"), an electronic record of eight digitised human [DNA sequences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_sequences "DNA sequences"), was placed aboard the ISS.[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-abc-20081012-274)

After a 12-year hiatus, the first two wholly space tourism-dedicated private spaceflights to the ISS were undertaken. [Soyuz MS-20](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soyuz_MS-20 "Soyuz MS-20") launched in December 2021, carrying visiting Roscosmos cosmonaut [Alexander Misurkin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Misurkin "Alexander Misurkin") and two Japanese space tourists under the aegis of the private company [Space Adventures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Adventures "Space Adventures");[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-space_adventures-275)[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tass20200219-276) in April 2022, the company [Axiom Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Space "Axiom Space") chartered a [SpaceX Dragon 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon_2 "SpaceX Dragon 2") spacecraft and sent its own employee astronaut [Michael Lopez-Alegria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Lopez-Alegria "Michael Lopez-Alegria") and three space tourists to the ISS for [Axiom Mission 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Mission_1 "Axiom Mission 1"),[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-teslarati-1-277)[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-axiom-first-private-278)[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ax1overview-279) followed in May 2023 by one more tourist, [John Shoffner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Shoffner "John Shoffner"), alongside employee astronaut [Peggy Whitson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peggy_Whitson "Peggy Whitson") and two Saudi astronauts for the [Axiom Mission 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Mission_2 "Axiom Mission 2").[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Sheetz_2021-280)[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Ax-2_Overview-281)

### Fleet operations

Various crewed and uncrewed spacecraft have supported the station's activities. Flights to the ISS include 37 Space Shuttle, 91 Progress,[[f]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-282) 72 Soyuz, 5 [ATV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle "Automated Transfer Vehicle"), 9 [HTV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle "H-II Transfer Vehicle"), 2 [Boeing Starliner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_Starliner "Boeing Starliner"), 49 [SpaceX Dragon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon "SpaceX Dragon")[[g]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-283) and 20 [Cygnus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft) "Cygnus (spacecraft)") missions.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacecom-20210810-284)

There are currently eight docking ports for visiting spacecraft, with four additional ports installed but not yet put into service:[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-her-285)

1.   _[Harmony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmony\_(ISS\_module) "Harmony (ISS module)")_ forward (with [_PMA 2_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pressurized_Mating_Adapter "Pressurized Mating Adapter")&[_IDA 2_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Docking_Adapter "International Docking Adapter"))
2.   _Harmony_ zenith (with [_PMA 3_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pressurized_Mating_Adapter "Pressurized Mating Adapter")&[_IDA 3_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Docking_Adapter "International Docking Adapter"))
3.   _Harmony_ nadir ([CBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_Berthing_Mechanism "Common Berthing Mechanism") port)
4.   _[Unity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unity\_(ISS\_module) "Unity (ISS module)")_ nadir ([CBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_Berthing_Mechanism "Common Berthing Mechanism") port)
5.   _[Prichal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prichal\_(ISS\_module) "Prichal (ISS module)")_ aft[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Prichal-286)
6.   _Prichal_ forward[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Prichal-286)
7.   _Prichal_ nadir
8.   _Prichal_ port[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Prichal-286)
9.   _Prichal_ starboard[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Prichal-286)
10.   _[Poisk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisk\_(ISS\_module) "Poisk (ISS module)")_ zenith
11.   _[Rassvet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rassvet\_(ISS\_module) "Rassvet (ISS module)")_ nadir
12.   _[Zvezda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zvezda\_(ISS\_module) "Zvezda (ISS module)")_ aft

[Forward](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_ship_directions "List of ship directions") ports are at the front of the station in its usual orientation and direction of travel. [Aft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_ship_directions "List of ship directions") is the opposite, at the rear. [Nadir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nadir "Nadir") points toward Earth, while [zenith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zenith "Zenith") points away from it. [Port](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_and_starboard "Port and starboard") is to the left and [starboard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Port_and_starboard "Port and starboard") to the right when one's feet are toward Earth and one is facing forward, in the direction of travel.

Cargo spacecraft that will perform an orbital re-boost of the station will typically dock at an aft, forward or nadir-facing port.

#### Crewed

As of 4 August 2025, a total of 290 individuals from 26 countries had visited the International Space Station (ISS), including both government-sponsored astronauts and privately funded spaceflight participants. The United States accounted for 170 of these visitors, followed by Russia with 64, Japan with 11, and [Canada](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada "Canada") with 9. [Italy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italy "Italy") had 6 visitors, while [France](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/France "France") and [Germany](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Germany "Germany") had each sent 4. [Saudi Arabia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia"), [Sweden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sweden "Sweden"), and the [United Arab Emirates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates") each had 2 individuals visit the ISS. One person had traveled to the ISS from each of the following countries: [Belarus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belarus "Belarus"), [Belgium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Belgium "Belgium"), [Brazil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazil "Brazil"), [Denmark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denmark "Denmark"), [Hungary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungary "Hungary"), [India](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India "India"), [Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel "Israel"), [Kazakhstan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kazakhstan "Kazakhstan"), [Malaysia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malaysia "Malaysia"), the [Netherlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Netherlands "Netherlands"), [Poland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poland "Poland"), [South Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa "South Africa"), [South Korea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Korea "South Korea"), [Spain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spain "Spain"), [Turkey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkey "Turkey"), and the [United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom").[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASA_Visitor_Count_2023-287)
