#### Uncrewed

Uncrewed spaceflights are made primarily to deliver cargo, however several Russian modules have also docked to the outpost following uncrewed launches. Resupply missions typically use the Russian [Progress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft) "Progress (spacecraft)") spacecraft, former European [ATVs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle "Automated Transfer Vehicle"), Japanese [Kounotori](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kounotori_(spacecraft) "Kounotori (spacecraft)") vehicles, and the American [Dragon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon "SpaceX Dragon") and [Cygnus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft) "Cygnus (spacecraft)") spacecraft.

#### Currently docked/berthed

[![Image 65](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/May_23%2C_2025_International_Space_Station_Configuration.png/250px-May_23%2C_2025_International_Space_Station_Configuration.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:May_23,_2025_International_Space_Station_Configuration.png)

Rendering of the ISS and visiting vehicles as of 23 May 2025. [Live link at nasa.gov](https://www.nasa.gov/international-space-station/space-station-visiting-vehicles/)

_All dates are [UTC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UTC "UTC"). Departure dates are the earliest possible (NET) and may change._

#### Scheduled missions

_All dates are [UTC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UTC "UTC"). Launch dates are the earliest possible (NET) and may change._

#### Docking and berthing of spacecraft

[![Image 66](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Progress_M-14M.jpg/250px-Progress_M-14M.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Progress_M-14M.jpg)

The [Progress M-14M](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_M-14M "Progress M-14M") resupply vehicle approaching the ISS in 2012. More than 50 unpiloted [Progress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft) "Progress (spacecraft)") spacecraft have delivered supplies during the lifetime of the station.

The Russian spacecraft and can autonomously rendezvous and dock with the station without human intervention. Once within approximately 200 kilometres (120 mi), the spacecraft begins receiving radio signals from the [Kurs docking navigation system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurs_(docking_navigation_system) "Kurs (docking navigation system)") on the station. As the spacecraft nears the station, laser-based optical equipment precisely aligns the craft with the docking port and controls the final approach. While the crew on the ISS and spacecraft monitor the procedure, their role is primarily supervisory, with intervention limited to issuing abort commands in emergencies. Although initial development costs were substantial, the system's reliability and standardized components have yielded significant cost reductions for subsequent missions.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-289)

The American [SpaceX Dragon 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon_2 "SpaceX Dragon 2") cargo and crewed spacecraft can autonomously rendezvous and dock with the station without human intervention. However, on crewed Dragon missions, the astronauts have the capability to intervene and fly the vehicle manually.[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-290)

[![Image 67](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg/250px-ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg)

Japan's [Kounotori 4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kounotori_4 "Kounotori 4") berthing

Other [automated cargo spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automated_cargo_spacecraft "Automated cargo spacecraft") typically use a semi-automated process when arriving and departing from the station. These spacecraft are instructed to approach and park near the station. Once the crew on board the station is ready, the spacecraft is commanded to come close to the station, so that it can be grappled by an astronaut using the [Mobile Servicing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_Servicing_System "Mobile Servicing System") robotic arm. The final mating of the spacecraft to the station is achieved using the robotic arm (a process known as berthing). Spacecraft using this semi-automated process include the American [Cygnus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft) "Cygnus (spacecraft)") and the Japanese [HTV-X](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTV-X "HTV-X"). The now-retired American [SpaceX Dragon 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon_1 "SpaceX Dragon 1"), European [ATV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle "Automated Transfer Vehicle") and Japanese [HTV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle "H-II Transfer Vehicle") also used this process.

#### Launch and docking windows

Prior to a spacecraft's docking to the ISS, navigation and attitude control ([GNC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guidance,_navigation,_and_control "Guidance, navigation, and control")) is handed over to the ground control of the spacecraft's country of origin. GNC is set to allow the station to drift in space, rather than fire its thrusters or turn using gyroscopes. The solar panels of the station are turned edge-on to the incoming spacecraft, so residue from its thrusters does not damage the cells. Before its retirement, Shuttle launches were often given priority over Soyuz, with occasional priority given to Soyuz arrivals carrying crew and time-critical cargoes, such as biological experiment materials.[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-291)

### Repairs

[![Image 68](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png/500px-ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png)

Spare parts are called [ORUs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_replacement_unit "Orbital replacement unit"); some are externally stored on pallets called [ELCs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExPRESS_Logistics_Carrier "ExPRESS Logistics Carrier") and [ESPs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/External_stowage_platform "External stowage platform").

[![Image 69: Two black and orange solar arrays, shown uneven and with a large tear visible. A crew member in a spacesuit, attached to the end of a robotic arm, holds a latticework between two solar sails.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg/250px-STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg)

While anchored on the end of the [Orbiter Boom Sensor System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiter_Boom_Sensor_System "Orbiter Boom Sensor System") during [STS-120](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-120 "STS-120"), astronaut [Scott Parazynski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scott_Parazynski "Scott Parazynski") performs makeshift repairs to a US solar array that was damaged during unfolding

[![Image 70](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg/250px-Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg)

[Mike Hopkins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_S._Hopkins "Michael S. Hopkins") during a spacewalk

[Orbital Replacement Units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_Replacement_Unit "Orbital Replacement Unit") (ORUs) are spare parts that can be readily replaced when a unit either passes its design life or fails. Examples of ORUs are pumps, storage tanks, controller boxes, antennas, and battery units. Some units can be replaced using robotic arms. Most are stored outside the station, either on small pallets called [ExPRESS Logistics Carriers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ExPRESS_Logistics_Carrier "ExPRESS Logistics Carrier") (ELCs) or share larger platforms called [External Stowage Platforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/External_Stowage_Platform "External Stowage Platform") (ESPs) which also hold science experiments. Both kinds of pallets provide electricity for many parts that could be damaged by the cold of space and require heating. The larger logistics carriers also have local area network (LAN) connections for telemetry to connect experiments. A heavy emphasis on stocking the USOS with ORU's occurred around 2011, before the end of the NASA shuttle programme, as its commercial replacements, Cygnus and Dragon, carry one tenth to one quarter the payload.

Unexpected problems and failures have impacted the station's assembly time-line and work schedules leading to periods of reduced capabilities and, in some cases, could have forced abandonment of the station for safety reasons. Serious problems include an air leak from the USOS in 2004,[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nbc-20040106-292) the venting of fumes from an _[Elektron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elektron\_(ISS) "Elektron (ISS)")_ oxygen generator in 2006,[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20060918-293) and the failure of the computers in the ROS in 2007 during [STS-117](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-117 "STS-117") that left the station without thruster, _Elektron_, _[Vozdukh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISS\_ECLSS#Vozdukh "ISS ECLSS")_ and other environmental control system operations. In the latter case, the root cause was found to be condensation inside electrical connectors leading to a short circuit.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-toledo-blade-20081004-294)

During STS-120 in 2007 and following the relocation of the P6 truss and solar arrays, it was noted during unfurling that the solar array had torn and was not deploying properly.[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Astronauts_notice_tear_in_solar_panel-295) An EVA was carried out by [Scott Parazynski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scott_E._Parazynski "Scott E. Parazynski"), assisted by [Douglas Wheelock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Douglas_H._Wheelock "Douglas H. Wheelock"). Extra precautions were taken to reduce the risk of electric shock, as the repairs were carried out with the solar array exposed to sunlight.[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Space_Station-296) The issues with the array were followed in the same year by problems with the starboard Solar Alpha Rotary Joint (SARJ), which rotates the arrays on the starboard side of the station. Excessive vibration and high-current spikes in the array drive motor were noted, resulting in a decision to substantially curtail motion of the starboard SARJ until the cause was understood. Inspections during EVAs on STS-120 and [STS-123](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-123 "STS-123") showed extensive contamination from metallic shavings and debris in the large drive gear and confirmed damage to the large metallic bearing surfaces, so the joint was locked to prevent further damage.[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-joint-update-297)[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-iss-joint-investigation-298) Repairs to the joints were carried out during [STS-126](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-126 "STS-126") with lubrication and the replacement of 11 out of 12 trundle bearings on the joint.[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-299)[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20081118-300)

In September 2008, damage to the S1 radiator was first noticed in Soyuz imagery. The problem was initially not thought to be serious.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Radiator-301) The imagery showed that the surface of one sub-panel had peeled back from the underlying central structure, possibly because of micro-meteoroid or debris impact. On 15 May 2009, the damaged radiator panel's ammonia tubing was mechanically shut off from the rest of the cooling system by the computer-controlled closure of a valve. The same valve was then used to vent the ammonia from the damaged panel, eliminating the possibility of an ammonia leak.[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Radiator-301) It is also known that a Service Module thruster cover struck the S1 radiator after being jettisoned during an EVA in 2008, but its effect, if any, has not been determined.

In the early hours of 1 August 2010, a failure in cooling Loop A (starboard side), one of two external cooling loops, left the station with only half of its normal cooling capacity and zero redundancy in some systems.[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-SpaceFlightNow1007_31-302)[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-iss-report-20100801-303)[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-304) The problem appeared to be in the ammonia pump module that circulates the ammonia cooling fluid. Several subsystems, including two of the four CMGs, were shut down.

Planned operations on the ISS were interrupted through a series of EVAs to address the cooling system issue. A first EVA on 7 August 2010, to replace the failed pump module, was not fully completed because of an ammonia leak in one of four quick-disconnects. A second EVA on 11 August removed the failed pump module.[[297]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20100810-305)[[298]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20100811-306) A third EVA was required to restore Loop A to normal functionality.[[299]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sfn-20100811-307)[[300]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20100818-308)

The USOS's cooling system is largely built by the US company Boeing,[[301]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacecom-20100802-309) which is also the manufacturer of the failed pump.[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-SpaceFlightNow1007_31-302)

The four Main Bus Switching Units (MBSUs, located in the S0 truss), control the routing of power from the four solar array wings to the rest of the ISS. Each MBSU has two power channels that feed 160V DC from the arrays to two DC-to-DC power converters (DDCUs) that supply the 124V power used in the station. In late 2011, MBSU-1 ceased responding to commands or sending data confirming its health. While still routing power correctly, it was scheduled to be swapped out at the next available EVA. A spare MBSU was already on board, but a 30 August 2012 EVA failed to be completed when a bolt being tightened to finish installation of the spare unit jammed before the electrical connection was secured.[[302]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20120830-310) The loss of MBSU-1 limited the station to 75% of its normal power capacity, requiring minor limitations in normal operations until the problem could be addressed.

On 5 September 2012, in a second six-hour EVA, astronauts Sunita Williams and Akihiko Hoshide successfully replaced MBSU-1 and restored the ISS to 100% power.[[303]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spaceref-20120905-311)

On 24 December 2013, astronauts installed a new ammonia pump for the station's cooling system. The faulty cooling system had failed earlier in the month, halting many of the station's science experiments. Astronauts had to brave a "mini blizzard" of ammonia while installing the new pump. It was only the second Christmas Eve spacewalk in NASA history.[[304]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-312)

### Mission control centres

The components of the ISS are operated and monitored by their respective space agencies at [mission control centres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mission_control_centre "Mission control centre") across the globe, primarily the [Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center "Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center") in Houston and the [RKA Mission Control Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RKA_Mission_Control_Center "RKA Mission Control Center") (TsUP) in Moscow, with support from [Tsukuba Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tsukuba_Space_Center "Tsukuba Space Center") in Japan, [Payload Operations and Integration Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Payload_Operations_and_Integration_Center "Payload Operations and Integration Center") in Huntsville, Alabama, U.S., [Columbus Control Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbus_Control_Center "Columbus Control Center") in Munich, Germany and [Mobile Servicing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_Servicing_System "Mobile Servicing System") Control at the [Canadian Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency "Canadian Space Agency")'s headquarters in [Saint-Hubert, Quebec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint-Hubert,_Quebec "Saint-Hubert, Quebec").

Orbit, debris and visibility
----------------------------

### Altitude and orbital inclination

[![Image 71](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Altitude_of_International_Space_Station.svg/250px-Altitude_of_International_Space_Station.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Altitude_of_International_Space_Station.svg)

Graph showing the changing altitude of the ISS from November 1998 until November 2018

[![Image 72](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Animation_of_International_Space_Station_trajectory.gif/250px-Animation_of_International_Space_Station_trajectory.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Animation_of_International_Space_Station_trajectory.gif)

Animation of ISS orbit from 14 September 2018 to 14 November 2018. Earth is not shown.

The ISS is currently maintained in a nearly circular orbit with a minimum mean altitude of 370 km (230 mi) and a maximum of 460 km (290 mi),[[305]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-iss-overview-313) in the centre of the [thermosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermosphere "Thermosphere"), at an [inclination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inclination "Inclination") of 51.6 degrees to Earth's equator[[306]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-314) with an eccentricity of 0.0002267.[[307]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-315)[_[needs update](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual\_of\_Style/Dates\_and\_numbers#Chronological\_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_] This orbit was selected because it is the lowest inclination that can be directly reached by Russian Soyuz and Progress spacecraft launched from [Baikonur Cosmodrome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome "Baikonur Cosmodrome") at 46° N latitude without overflying China or dropping spent rocket stages in inhabited areas.[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-MCC_Answer-316)[[309]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-317) It travels at an average speed of 28,000 kilometres per hour (17,000 mph), and completes 15.5 orbits per day (93 minutes per orbit).[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-heavens-above-4)[[310]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-tracking-318) The station's altitude was allowed to fall around the time of each NASA shuttle flight to permit heavier loads to be transferred to the station. After the retirement of the shuttle, the nominal orbit of the space station was raised in altitude (from about 350 km to about 400 km).[[311]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20110620-319)[[312]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Popular_Mechanics-320) Other, more frequent supply spacecraft do not require this adjustment as they are substantially higher performance vehicles.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Worldbook_at_NASA-29)[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa.gov-iss-environment-321)

Atmospheric drag reduces the altitude by about 2 km a month on average. Orbital boosting can be performed by the station's two main engines on the _Zvezda_ service module, or Russian or European spacecraft docked to _Zvezda_'s aft port. The Automated Transfer Vehicle is constructed with the possibility of adding a [second docking port](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle#ATV_evolution_proposals "Automated Transfer Vehicle") to its aft end, allowing other craft to dock and boost the station. It takes approximately two orbits (three hours) for the boost to a higher altitude to be completed.[[313]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa.gov-iss-environment-321) Maintaining ISS altitude uses about 7.5 tonnes of chemical fuel per annum[[314]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-newscientist.com-322) at an annual cost of about $210 million.[[315]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-aaESummary20100124-323)

[![Image 73](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg/330px-ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg)

Orbits of the ISS, shown in April 2013

The Russian Orbital Segment contains the Data Management System, which handles Guidance, Navigation and Control (ROS GNC) for the entire station.[[316]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Navigation-324) Initially, _Zarya_, the first module of the station, controlled the station until a short time after the Russian service module _Zvezda_ docked and was transferred control. _Zvezda_ contains the ESA built DMS-R Data Management System.[[317]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-EsaComputer-325) Using two fault-tolerant computers (FTC), _Zvezda_ computes the station's position and orbital trajectory using redundant Earth horizon sensors, Solar horizon sensors as well as Sun and star trackers. The FTCs each contain three identical processing units working in parallel and provide advanced fault-masking by majority voting.

### Orientation

_Zvezda_ uses gyroscopes ([reaction wheels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reaction_wheel "Reaction wheel")) and thrusters to turn itself. Gyroscopes do not require propellant; instead they use electricity to 'store' momentum in flywheels by turning in the opposite direction to the station's movement. The USOS has its own computer-controlled gyroscopes to handle its extra mass. When gyroscopes ['saturate'](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Control_moment_gyroscope#Saturation "Control moment gyroscope"), thrusters are used to cancel out the stored momentum. In February 2005, during Expedition 10, an incorrect command was sent to the station's computer, using about 14 kilograms of propellant before the fault was noticed and fixed. When attitude control computers in the ROS and USOS fail to communicate properly, this can result in a rare 'force fight' where the ROS GNC computer must ignore the USOS counterpart, which itself has no thrusters.[[318]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-326)[[319]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-iss-report-05-07-327)[[320]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-dynamics-report-328)

Docked spacecraft can also be used to maintain station attitude, such as for troubleshooting or during the [installation of the S3/S4 truss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-117 "STS-117"), which provides electrical power and data interfaces for the station's electronics.[[321]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nsf-20070614-329)

### Orbital debris threats

The low altitudes at which the ISS orbits are also home to a variety of space debris,[[322]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-330) including spent rocket stages, defunct satellites, explosion fragments (including materials from [anti-satellite weapon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-satellite_weapon "Anti-satellite weapon") tests), paint flakes, slag from solid rocket motors, and coolant released by [US-A](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US-A "US-A") nuclear-powered satellites. These objects, in addition to natural [micrometeoroids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micrometeoroid "Micrometeoroid"),[[323]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-331) are a significant threat. Objects large enough to destroy the station can be tracked, and therefore are not as dangerous as smaller debris.[[324]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NSFdebris-332)[[325]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-micrometeoroid-report-333) Objects too small to be detected by optical and radar instruments, from approximately 1 cm down to microscopic size, number in the trillions. Despite their small size, some of these objects are a threat because of their [kinetic energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinetic_energy "Kinetic energy") and direction in relation to the station. Spacewalking crew in spacesuits are also at risk of suit damage and consequent [exposure to vacuum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_exposure "Space exposure").[[326]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-debrisdecomp-334)

Ballistic panels, also called micrometeorite shielding, are incorporated into the station to protect pressurised sections and critical systems. The type and thickness of these panels depend on their predicted exposure to damage. The station's shields and structure have different designs on the ROS and the USOS. On the USOS, [Whipple Shields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whipple_Shield "Whipple Shield") are used. The US segment modules consist of an inner layer made from 1.5–5.0 cm-thick (0.59–1.97 in) [aluminium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2219_aluminium_alloy "2219 aluminium alloy"), a 10 cm-thick (3.9 in) intermediate layers of [Kevlar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kevlar "Kevlar") and Nextel (a ceramic fabric),[[327]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20040716-335) and an outer layer of [stainless steel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stainless_steel "Stainless steel"), which causes objects to shatter into a cloud before hitting the hull, thereby spreading the energy of impact. On the ROS, a [carbon fibre reinforced polymer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fibre_reinforced_polymer "Carbon fibre reinforced polymer") honeycomb screen is spaced from the hull, an aluminium honeycomb screen is spaced from that, with a screen-vacuum thermal insulation covering, and glass cloth over the top.[[328]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-roscomos-iss-336)

Space debris is tracked remotely from the ground, and the station crew can be notified.[[329]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-debris-package-337) If necessary, thrusters on the Russian Orbital Segment can alter the station's orbital altitude, avoiding the debris. These [Debris Avoidance Manoeuvres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Debris_Avoidance_Manoeuvre "Debris Avoidance Manoeuvre") (DAMs) are not uncommon, taking place if computational models show the debris will approach within a certain threat distance. Ten DAMs had been performed by the end of 2009.[[330]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-newscientist-20090316-338)[[331]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ODOct08-339)[[332]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-340) Usually, an increase in orbital velocity of the order of 1 m/s is used to raise the orbit by one or two kilometres. If necessary, the altitude can also be lowered, although such a manoeuvre wastes propellant.[[331]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ODOct08-339)[[333]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa-20080828-341) If a threat from orbital debris is identified too late for a DAM to be safely conducted, the station crew close all the hatches aboard the station and retreat into their spacecraft in order to be able to evacuate in the event the station was seriously damaged by the debris. Partial station evacuations have occurred on 13 March 2009, 28 June 2011, 24 March 2012, 16 June 2015,[[334]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-bbc-20120324-342) November 2021,[[335]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-reuters-20211203-343) and [27 June 2024](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Resurs-P_No.1 "Resurs-P No.1").[[336]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-cnn_27Jun2024-344)

The November 2021 evacuation was caused by a Russian anti-satellite [weapon test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosmos_1408 "Kosmos 1408").[[337]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-verge-20211115-345)[[338]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-BBC_16Nov21-346) NASA administrator [Bill Nelson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Nelson "Bill Nelson") said it was unthinkable that Russia would endanger the lives of everyone on ISS, including their own cosmonauts.[[339]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-cnn_15Nov21-347)

*   [![Image 74: A 7-gram object (shown in centre) shot at 7 km/s (23,000 ft/s), the orbital velocity of the ISS, made this 15 cm (5.9 in) crater in a solid block of aluminium.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg/250px-SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg "A 7-gram object (shown in centre) shot at 7 km/s (23,000 ft/s), the orbital velocity of the ISS, made this 15 cm (5.9 in) crater in a solid block of aluminium.")

*   [![Image 76: Example of risk management: A NASA model showing areas at high risk from impact for the International Space Station](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/ISS_impact_risk.jpg/250px-ISS_impact_risk.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS_impact_risk.jpg "Example of risk management: A NASA model showing areas at high risk from impact for the International Space Station")

Example of [risk management](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Risk_management "Risk management"): A NASA model showing areas at high risk from impact for the International Space Station

### Visibility from Earth

The ISS is visible in the [sky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sky "Sky") to the [naked eye](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naked_eye "Naked eye") as a visibly moving, bright white dot, when crossing the sky and being illuminated by the Sun, during [twilight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twilight "Twilight"), the hours after sunset and before sunrise, when the station remains sunlit, outside of [Earth's shadow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_shadow "Earth's shadow"), but the ground and sky are dark.[[340]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Price2005-348) It crosses the skies at latitudes between the [polar regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_region "Polar region").[[341]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-d361-349) Depending on the path it takes across the sky, the time it takes the station to move across the horizon or from one to the other may be short or up to 10 minutes, while likely being only visible part of that time because of it moving into or out of Earth's shadow. It then returns around every 90 minutes, with the time of the day that it crosses the sky shifting over the course of some weeks, and therefore before returning to twilight and visible illumination.

Because of the size of its reflective surface area, the ISS is the brightest artificial object in the sky (excluding other [satellite flares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite_flare "Satellite flare")), with an approximate maximum [magnitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_magnitude "Apparent magnitude") of −4 when in sunlight and overhead (similar to [Venus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus "Venus")), and a maximum [angular size](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_size "Angular size") of 63 arcseconds.[[342]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Angular_size-350)

Tools are provided by a number of websites such as [Heavens-Above](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heavens-Above "Heavens-Above") (see [_Live viewing_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#Live_viewing) below) as well as [smartphone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smartphone "Smartphone") applications that use [orbital data](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ephemeris "Ephemeris") and the observer's longitude and latitude to indicate when the ISS will be visible (weather permitting), where the station will appear to rise, the altitude above the horizon it will reach and the duration of the pass before the station disappears either by setting below the horizon or entering into Earth's shadow.[[343]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-see-351)[[344]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-352)[[345]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-353)[[346]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-daytime_visibility-354)

In November 2012 NASA launched its "Spot the Station" service, which sends people text and email alerts when the station is due to fly above their town.[[347]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-355) The station is visible from 95% of the inhabited land on Earth, but is not visible from extreme northern or southern latitudes.[[308]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-MCC_Answer-316)

Under specific conditions, the ISS can be observed at night on five consecutive orbits. Those conditions are 1) a mid-latitude observer location, 2) near the time of the solstice with 3) the ISS passing in the direction of the pole from the observer near midnight local time. The three photos show the first, middle and last of the five passes on 5–6 June 2014.
