#### Astrophotography

[![Image 81](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg/250px-Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg)

The ISS and HTV photographed from Earth by [Ralf Vandebergh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralf_Vandebergh "Ralf Vandebergh")

Using a telescope-mounted camera to photograph the station is a popular hobby for astronomers,[[348]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-hobbyspace-satwatching-356) while using a mounted camera to photograph the Earth and stars is a popular hobby for crew.[[349]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-20030324-357) The use of a telescope or binoculars allows viewing of the ISS during daylight hours.[[350]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-358)

[![Image 82](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Composite_of_6_photos_of_the_ISS_transiting_the_gibbous_Moon.jpg/250px-Composite_of_6_photos_of_the_ISS_transiting_the_gibbous_Moon.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Composite_of_6_photos_of_the_ISS_transiting_the_gibbous_Moon.jpg)

Composite of six photos of the ISS transiting the [gibbous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_phase "Lunar phase") Moon

[Transits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transit_(astronomy) "Transit (astronomy)") of the ISS in front of the Sun, particularly during an [eclipse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_eclipse "Solar eclipse") (and so the Earth, Sun, Moon, and ISS are all positioned approximately in a single line) are of particular interest for amateur astronomers.[[351]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-359)[[352]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-wired-20110105-360)

Environment, safety and crew health
-----------------------------------

### Environment

#### Freefall environment

Gravity at the altitude of the ISS is approximately 90% as strong as at Earth's surface, but objects in orbit are in a continuous state of [freefall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_fall "Free fall"), resulting in an apparent state of [weightlessness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weightlessness "Weightlessness").[[353]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-whatismicrogravity-361) This perceived weightlessness is disturbed by five effects:[[354]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-gravity-362)

*   Drag from the residual atmosphere.
*   Vibration from the movements of mechanical systems and the crew.
*   Actuation of the on-board attitude [control moment gyroscopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Control_moment_gyroscope "Control moment gyroscope").
*   [Thruster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rocket_engine "Rocket engine") firings for attitude or orbital changes.
*   [Gravity-gradient effects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity-gradient_stabilization "Gravity-gradient stabilization"), also known as [tidal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_force "Tidal force") effects. Items at different locations within the ISS would, if not attached to the station, follow slightly different orbits. Being mechanically connected, these items experience small forces that keep the station moving as a [rigid body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rigid_body "Rigid body").

#### Radiation

Video of the [Aurora Australis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora_Australis "Aurora Australis"), taken by the crew of [Expedition 28](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_28 "Expedition 28") on an ascending pass from south of [Madagascar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar "Madagascar") to just north of Australia over the Indian Ocean

The ISS is partially protected from the space environment by [Earth's magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field "Earth's magnetic field"). From an average distance of about 70,000 km (43,000 mi) from the Earth's surface, depending on Solar activity, the [magnetosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetosphere "Magnetosphere") begins to deflect [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind") around Earth and the space station. [Solar flares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_flare "Solar flare") are still a hazard to the crew, who may receive only a few minutes warning. In 2005, during the initial "proton storm" of an X-3 class solar flare, the crew of [Expedition 10](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_10 "Expedition 10") took shelter in a more heavily shielded part of the ROS designed for this purpose.[[355]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacecom-20060223-363)[[356]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-364)

Subatomic charged particles, primarily [protons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton "Proton") from cosmic rays and solar wind, are normally absorbed by Earth's atmosphere. When they interact in sufficient quantity, their effect is visible to the naked eye in a phenomenon called an [aurora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurora "Aurora"). Outside Earth's atmosphere, ISS crews are exposed to approximately one [millisievert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millisievert "Millisievert") each day (about a year's worth of natural exposure on Earth), resulting in a higher risk of cancer. Radiation can penetrate living tissue and damage the DNA and [chromosomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromosome "Chromosome") of [lymphocytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphocyte "Lymphocyte"); being central to the [immune system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_system "Immune system"), any damage to these cells could contribute to the lower [immunity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immunity_(medical) "Immunity (medical)") experienced by astronauts. Radiation has also been linked to a higher incidence of [cataracts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cataract "Cataract") in astronauts. Protective shielding and medications may lower the risks to an acceptable level.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-JCB-47)

Radiation levels on the ISS are between 12 and 28.8 milli rads per day,[[357]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Forbes_2018-365) about five times greater than those experienced by airline passengers and crew, as Earth's electromagnetic field provides almost the same level of protection against solar and other types of radiation in low Earth orbit as in the stratosphere. For example, on a 12-hour flight, an airline passenger would experience 0.1 millisieverts of radiation, or a rate of 0.2 millisieverts per day; this is one fifth the rate experienced by an astronaut in LEO. Additionally, airline passengers experience this level of radiation for a few hours of flight, while the ISS crew are exposed for their whole stay on board the station.[[358]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-366)

#### Microbiological environmental hazards

Hazardous molds that can foul air and water filters may develop aboard space stations. They can produce acids that degrade metal, glass, and rubber. They can also be harmful to the crew's health. Microbiological hazards have led to a development of the [LOCAD-PTS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LOCAD#Portable_Test_System "LOCAD") (a portable test system) which identifies common bacteria and molds faster than standard methods of [culturing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_culture "Cell culture"), which may require a sample to be sent back to Earth.[[359]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-367) Researchers in 2018 reported, after detecting the presence of five _[Enterobacter bugandensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enterobacter "Enterobacter")_ bacterial strains on the ISS (none of which are [pathogenic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogen "Pathogen") to humans), that microorganisms on the ISS should be carefully monitored to continue assuring a medically healthy environment for astronauts.[[360]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-EA-20181122-368)[[361]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-BMC-20181123-369)

Contamination on space stations can be prevented by reduced humidity, and by using paint that contains mold-killing chemicals, as well as the use of antiseptic solutions. All materials used in the ISS are tested for resistance against [fungi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fungi "Fungi").[[362]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-370) Since 2016, a series of ESA-sponsored experiments have been conducted to test the anti-bacterial properties of various materials, with the goal of developing "smart surfaces" that mitigate bacterial growth in multiple ways, using the best method for a particular circumstance. Dubbed "Microbial Aerosol Tethering on Innovative Surfaces" (MATISS), the programme involves deployment of small plaques containing an array of glass squares covered with different test coatings. They remain on the station for six months before being returned to earth for analysis.[[363]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-eoportal-iss-matiss-371) The most recent and final experiment of the series was launched on 5 June 2023 aboard the [SpaceX CRS-28](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-28 "SpaceX CRS-28") cargo mission to ISS, comprising four plaques. Whereas previous experiments in the series were limited to analysis by [light microsocopy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microscopy "Microscopy"), this experiment uses quartz glass made of pure silica, which will allow [spectrographic analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectroscopy "Spectroscopy"). Two of the plaques were returned after eight months and the remaining two after 16 months.[[364]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-arstechnica-20230608-372)

In April 2019, NASA reported that a comprehensive study had been conducted into the microorganisms and fungi present on the ISS. The experiment was performed over a period of 14 months on three different flight missions, and involved taking samples from 8 predefined locations inside the station, then returning them to earth for analysis. In prior experiments, analysis was limited to culture-based methods, thus overlooking microbes which cannot be grown in culture. The present study used [molecular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_diagnostics "Molecular diagnostics")-based methods in addition to culturing, resulting in a more complete catalog. The results may be useful in improving the health and safety conditions for astronauts, as well as better understanding other closed-in environments on Earth such as clean rooms used by the pharmaceutical and medical industries.[[365]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-EA-20190407-373)[[366]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-MBJ-20190408-374)

#### Noise

Space flight is not inherently quiet, with noise levels exceeding acoustic standards as far back as the [Apollo missions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_missions "Apollo missions").[[367]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-375)[[368]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-376) For this reason, NASA and the International Space Station international partners have developed [noise control](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noise_control "Noise control") and [hearing loss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hearing_loss "Hearing loss") prevention goals as part of the health program for crew members. Specifically, these goals have been the primary focus of the ISS Multilateral Medical Operations Panel (MMOP) Acoustics Subgroup since the first days of ISS assembly and operations.[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-MMOP_SSP_50260-377)[[370]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-iss-acoustics-378) The effort includes contributions from [acoustical engineers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acoustical_engineering "Acoustical engineering"), [audiologists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Audiologists "Audiologists"), [industrial hygienists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Industrial_hygienists "Industrial hygienists"), and physicians who comprise the subgroup's membership from NASA, Roscosmos, the European Space Agency (ESA), the Japanese Aerospace Exploration Agency (JAXA), and the Canadian Space Agency (CSA).

When compared to terrestrial environments, the noise levels experienced by astronauts and cosmonauts on the ISS may seem insignificant and typically occur at levels that would not be of major concern to the [Occupational Safety and Health Administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration "Occupational Safety and Health Administration") – rarely reaching 85 dBA. But crew members are exposed to these levels 24 hours a day, seven days a week, with current missions averaging six months in duration. These levels of noise also impose risks to crew health and performance in the form of sleep interference and communication, as well as reduced alarm [audibility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hearing "Hearing").

Over the 19 plus year history of the ISS, significant efforts have been put forth to limit and reduce noise levels on the ISS. During design and pre-flight activities, members of the Acoustic Subgroup have written acoustic limits and verification requirements, consulted to design and choose the quietest available payloads, and then conducted acoustic verification tests prior to launch.[[369]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-MMOP_SSP_50260-377): 5.7.3  During spaceflights, the Acoustics Subgroup has assessed each ISS module's in flight sound levels, produced by a large number of vehicle and science experiment noise sources, to assure compliance with strict acoustic standards. The acoustic environment on ISS changed when additional modules were added during its construction, and as additional spacecraft arrive at the ISS. The Acoustics Subgroup has responded to this dynamic operations schedule by successfully designing and employing acoustic covers, absorptive materials, [noise barriers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noise_barrier "Noise barrier"), and vibration isolators to reduce noise levels. Moreover, when pumps, fans, and ventilation systems age and show increased noise levels, this Acoustics Subgroup has guided ISS managers to replace the older, noisier instruments with quiet fan and pump technologies, significantly reducing [ambient noise levels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ambient_noise_level "Ambient noise level").

NASA has adopted most-conservative damage risk criteria (based on recommendations from the [National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health "National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health") and the [World Health Organization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Health_Organization "World Health Organization")), in order to protect all crew members. The MMOP Acoustics Subgroup has adjusted its approach to managing noise risks in this unique environment by applying, or modifying, terrestrial approaches for hearing loss prevention to set these conservative limits. One innovative approach has been NASA's Noise Exposure Estimation Tool (NEET), in which noise exposures are calculated in a task-based approach to determine the need for [hearing protection devices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hearing_protection_device "Hearing protection device") (HPDs). Guidance for use of HPDs, either mandatory use or recommended, is then documented in the Noise Hazard Inventory, and posted for crew reference during their missions. The Acoustics Subgroup also tracks spacecraft noise exceedances, applies [engineering controls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engineering_controls "Engineering controls"), and recommends hearing protective devices to reduce crew noise exposures. Finally, hearing thresholds are monitored on-orbit, during missions.

There have been no persistent mission-related hearing threshold shifts among US Orbital Segment crewmembers (JAXA, CSA, ESA, NASA) during what is approaching 20 years of ISS mission operations, or nearly 175,000 work hours. In 2020, the MMOP Acoustics Subgroup received the [Safe-In-Sound Award](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Safe-In-Sound_Award "Safe-In-Sound Award") for Innovation for their combined efforts to mitigate any health effects of noise.[[371]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-379)

#### Fire and toxic gases

An onboard fire or a toxic gas leak are other potential hazards. Ammonia is used in the external radiators of the station and could potentially leak into the pressurised modules.[[372]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-380)

### Overall health effects

On 12 April 2019, NASA reported medical results from the [Astronaut Twin Study](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ISS_year_long_mission "ISS year long mission"). Astronaut [Scott Kelly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scott_Kelly_(astronaut) "Scott Kelly (astronaut)") spent a year in space on the ISS, while [his identical twin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Kelly "Mark Kelly") spent the year on Earth. Several long-lasting changes were observed, including those related to alterations in [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") and [cognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognition "Cognition"), when one twin was compared with the other.[[373]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NYT-20190412-381)[[374]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-SCI-20190412-382)

In November 2019, researchers reported that astronauts experienced serious [blood flow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemodynamics "Hemodynamics") and [clot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thrombus "Thrombus") problems while on board the ISS, based on a six-month study of 11 healthy astronauts. The results may influence long-term spaceflight, including a mission to the planet Mars, according to the researchers.[[375]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-CNN-20191115-383)[[376]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-JAMA-20191113-384)

#### Stress

There is considerable evidence that [psychosocial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Psychosocial "Psychosocial") stressors are among the most important impediments to optimal crew morale and performance.[[377]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-385) Cosmonaut [Valery Ryumin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Valery_Ryumin "Valery Ryumin") wrote in his journal during a particularly difficult period on board the [_Salyut_ 6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salyut_6 "Salyut 6") space station: "All the conditions necessary for murder are met if you shut two men in a cabin measuring 18 feet by 20 [5.5 m × 6 m] and leave them together for two months."

NASA's interest in [psychological stress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stress_(psychological) "Stress (psychological)") caused by space travel, initially studied when their crewed missions began, was rekindled when astronauts joined cosmonauts on the Russian space station _Mir_. Common sources of stress in early US missions included maintaining high performance under public scrutiny and isolation from peers and family. The latter is still often a cause of stress on the ISS, such as when the mother of NASA astronaut [Daniel Tani](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_M._Tani "Daniel M. Tani") died in a car accident, and when Michael Fincke was forced to miss the birth of his second child.

A study of the longest spaceflight concluded that the first three weeks are a critical period where attention is adversely affected because of the demand to adjust to the extreme change of environment.[[378]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-386) ISS crew flights typically last about five to six months.

The ISS working environment includes further stress caused by living and working in cramped conditions with people from very different cultures who speak a different language. First-generation space stations had crews who spoke a single language; second- and third-generation stations have crew from many cultures who speak many languages. Astronauts must speak English and Russian, and knowing additional languages is even better.[[379]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-387)

Due to the lack of gravity, confusion often occurs. Even though there is no up and down in space, some crew members feel like they are oriented upside down. They may also have difficulty measuring distances. This can cause problems like getting lost inside the space station, pulling switches in the wrong direction or misjudging the speed of an approaching vehicle during docking.[[380]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-bbc-20141007-388)

#### Medical

[![Image 83: A man running on a treadmill, smiling at the camera, with bungee cords stretching down from his waistband to the sides of the treadmill](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg/250px-Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg)

Astronaut [Frank De Winne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_De_Winne "Frank De Winne"), attached to the [TVIS treadmill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treadmill_with_Vibration_Isolation_Stabilization "Treadmill with Vibration Isolation Stabilization") with bungee cords aboard the ISS

The [physiological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physiology "Physiology") effects of long-term weightlessness include [muscle atrophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muscle_atrophy "Muscle atrophy"), deterioration of the skeleton ([osteopenia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaceflight_osteopenia "Spaceflight osteopenia")), fluid redistribution, a slowing of the cardiovascular system, decreased production of red blood cells, balance disorders, and a weakening of the immune system. Lesser symptoms include loss of body mass, and puffiness of the face.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-JCB-47)

Sleep is regularly disturbed on the ISS because of mission demands, such as incoming or departing spacecraft. Sound levels in the station are unavoidably high. The atmosphere is unable to [thermosiphon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermosiphon "Thermosiphon") naturally, so fans are required at all times to process the air which would stagnate in the freefall (zero-G) environment.

To prevent some of the adverse effects on the body, the station is equipped with: two [TVIS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Treadmill_with_Vibration_Isolation_Stabilization "Treadmill with Vibration Isolation Stabilization") treadmills (including the COLBERT); the [ARED](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ARED "ARED") (Advanced Resistive Exercise Device), which enables various weightlifting exercises that add muscle without raising (or compensating for) the astronauts' reduced bone density;[[381]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-389) and a stationary bicycle. Each astronaut spends at least two hours per day exercising on the equipment.[[382]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ESALife-390)[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-391) Astronauts use bungee cords to strap themselves to the treadmill.[[384]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-392)[[385]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-393)

Life aboard
-----------

### Living quarters

[![Image 84](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg/250px-Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg)

Cosmonaut [Nikolai Budarin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolai_Budarin "Nikolai Budarin") at work inside the _[Zvezda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zvezda\_(ISS\_module) "Zvezda (ISS module)")_ service module crew quarters

The living and working space aboard the International Space Station (ISS) is larger than a six-bedroom house, equipped with seven private sleeping quarters, three bathrooms, two dining rooms, a gym, and a panoramic 360-degree-view bay window.[[386]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-394)

The station provides dedicated crew quarters for long-term crew members. Two "sleep stations" are located in the _Zvezda_ module, one in _Nauka_, and four in _Harmony_.[[387]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-roscomos-20210811-395)[[388]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-396)[[389]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-roscomos-nauka-booklet-397)[[390]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sae-08ICES-0222-398) These soundproof, person-sized booths offer privacy, ventilation, and basic amenities such as a sleeping bag, a reading lamp, a desktop, a shelf, and storage for personal items.[[382]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ESALife-390)[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-391)[[391]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-CSALife-399) The quarters in _Zvezda_ include a small window but have less ventilation and soundproofing.

Visiting crew members use tethered sleeping bags attached to available wall space. While it is possible to sleep floating freely, this is generally avoided to prevent collisions with sensitive equipment.[[392]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-SRLife-400) Proper ventilation is critical, as astronauts risk oxygen deprivation if exhaled carbon dioxide accumulates in a bubble around their heads.[[382]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ESALife-390)

The station’s lighting system is adjustable, allowing for dimming, switching off, and [colour temperature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Color_temperature "Color temperature") changes to support crew activities and rest.[[393]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-401)[[394]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nasa-stem-sleepscience-402)

### Crew activities

[![Image 85](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg/250px-Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg)

Engineer [Gregory Chamitoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gregory_Chamitoff "Gregory Chamitoff") looking out of a window

The ISS operates on [Coordinated Universal Time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time "Coordinated Universal Time") (UTC).[[395]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-bbc-iss-timezone-403) A typical day aboard the ISS begins at 06:00 with wake-up, post-sleep routines, and a morning inspection of the station. After breakfast, the crew holds a daily planning conference with Mission Control, starting work around 08:10. Morning tasks include scheduled exercise, scientific experiments, maintenance, or operational duties. Following a one-hour lunch break at 13:05, the crew resumes their afternoon schedule of work and exercise. Pre-sleep activities, including dinner and a crew conference, begin at 19:30, with the scheduled sleep period starting at 21:30.[[396]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:3-404)

The crew works approximately 10 hours on weekdays and 5 hours on Saturdays, with the remaining time allocated for relaxation or catching up on tasks. Free time often involves enjoying personal hobbies, communicating with family, or gazing out at Earth through the station’s windows.[[396]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:3-404) The crew can watch TV aboard the station.[[397]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-405)

When the Space Shuttle was operating, the ISS crew aligned with the shuttle crew's [Mission Elapsed Time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mission_Elapsed_Time "Mission Elapsed Time"), a flexible schedule based on the shuttle's launch.[[398]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-406)[[399]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-407)[[400]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-408)

To simulate night conditions, the station’s windows are covered during designated sleep periods, as the ISS experiences 16 sunrises and sunsets daily due to its orbital speed.

### Reflection and material culture

Reflection of individual and crew characteristics are found particularly in the decoration of the station and expressions in general, such as religion.[[401]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-z534-409) The latter has produced a certain material economy between the station and Russia in particular.[[402]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-q938-410)

The micro-society of the station, as well as wider society, and possibly the emergence of distinct station cultures,[[403]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-p157-411) is being studied by analyzing many aspects, from art to dust accumulation, as well as archaeologically how material of the ISS has been discarded.[[404]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-e121-412)

### Food and personal hygiene

[![Image 86](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Zvezda_toilet.jpg/120px-Zvezda_toilet.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Zvezda_toilet.jpg)

The space toilet in the _[Zvezda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zvezda\_(ISS\_module) "Zvezda (ISS module)")_ module in the Russian segment

[![Image 87](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Node_3_toilet.jpg/250px-Node_3_toilet.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Node_3_toilet.jpg)

The main toilet in the US Segment inside the _[Tranquility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tranquility\_(ISS\_module) "Tranquility (ISS module)")_ module

* Both toilets are a Russian design.

[![Image 88: Nine astronauts seated around a table covered in open cans of food strapped down to the table. In the background a selection of equipment is visible, as well as the salmon-coloured walls of the Unity node.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Meal_STS127.jpg/250px-Meal_STS127.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Meal_STS127.jpg)

The crews of [Expedition 20](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expedition_20 "Expedition 20") and [STS-127](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-127 "STS-127") enjoy a meal inside _Unity_.

[![Image 89](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/ISS-43_Food_table_in_the_Unity_module.jpg/250px-ISS-43_Food_table_in_the_Unity_module.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ISS-43_Food_table_in_the_Unity_module.jpg)

Main dining desk in Node 1

Fresh fruits and vegetables are grown in the ISS.

On the USOS, most of the food aboard is vacuum sealed in plastic bags; cans are rare because they are heavy and expensive to transport. Preserved food is not highly regarded by the crew and taste is reduced in microgravity,[[382]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ESALife-390) so efforts are taken to make the food more palatable, including using more spices than in regular cooking. The crew looks forward to the arrival of any spacecraft from Earth as they bring fresh fruit and vegetables. Care is taken that foods do not create crumbs, and liquid condiments are preferred over solid to avoid contaminating station equipment. Each crew member has individual food packages and cooks them in the [galley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galley_(kitchen) "Galley (kitchen)"), which has two food warmers, a refrigerator (added in November 2008), and a water dispenser that provides heated and unheated water.[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-391) Drinks are provided as dehydrated powder that is mixed with water before consumption.[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-391)[[391]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-CSALife-399) Drinks and soups are sipped from plastic bags with straws, while solid food is eaten with a knife and fork attached to a tray with magnets to prevent them from floating away. Any food that floats away, including crumbs, must be collected to prevent it from clogging the station's air filters and other equipment.[[391]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-CSALife-399)

Showers on space stations were introduced in the early 1970s on _Skylab_ and _Salyut_ 3.[[405]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-livingandworking-413): 139  By _Salyut_ 6, in the early 1980s, the crew complained of the complexity of showering in space, which was a monthly activity.[[406]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Portree1995-86-414) The ISS does not feature a shower; instead, crewmembers wash using a water jet and wet wipes, with soap dispensed from a toothpaste tube-like container. Crews are also provided with rinseless shampoo and edible toothpaste to save water.[[392]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-SRLife-400)[[407]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-415)

There are two [space toilets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_toilet "Space toilet") on the ISS, both of Russian design, located in _Zvezda_ and _Tranquility_.[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-391) These Waste and Hygiene Compartments use a fan-driven suction system similar to the Space Shuttle Waste Collection System. Astronauts first fasten themselves to the toilet seat, which is equipped with spring-loaded restraining bars to ensure a good seal.[[382]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ESALife-390) A lever operates a powerful fan and a suction hole slides open: the air stream carries the waste away. Solid waste is collected in individual bags which are stored in an aluminium container. Full containers are transferred to Progress spacecraft for disposal.[[383]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-391)[[408]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-416) Liquid waste is evacuated by a hose connected to the front of the toilet, with anatomically correct "urine funnel adapters" attached to the tube so that men and women can use the same toilet. [The diverted urine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urine_diversion "Urine diversion") is collected and transferred to the Water Recovery System, where it is recycled into drinking water.[[391]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-CSALife-399) In 2021, the arrival of the Nauka module also brought a third toilet to the ISS.[[409]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-417)

End of mission
--------------

Originally the ISS was planned to be a 15-year mission.[[410]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-s750-418) Therefore, an end of mission had been worked on,[[411]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-w234-419) but was several times postponed due to the success and support for the operation of the station.[[412]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-q972-420) As a result, the oldest modules of the ISS have been in orbit for more than 20 years, with their reliability having decreased.[[411]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-w234-419) It has been proposed to use funds elsewhere instead, for example for a return to the Moon.[[412]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-q972-420) According to the [Outer Space Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty "Outer Space Treaty"), the parties are legally responsible for all spacecraft or modules they launch.[[413]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-outerspacetreaty-421) An [unmaintained station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_station-keeping "Orbital station-keeping") would pose an [orbital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_debris "Space debris") and [re-entry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Re-entry "Re-entry") hazard.

Russia has stated that it plans to pull out of the ISS program after 2025.[[414]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-y941-422) However, Russian modules will provide [orbital station-keeping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_station-keeping "Orbital station-keeping") until 2028.[[411]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-w234-419)

The US planned in 2009 to deorbit the ISS in 2016.[[412]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-q972-420) But on 30 September 2015, Boeing's contract with NASA as prime contractor for the ISS was extended to 30 September 2020. Part of Boeing's services under the contract related to extending the station's primary structural hardware past 2020 to the end of 2028.[[415]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacedaily-20150930-423) In July 2018, the Space Frontier Act of 2018 was intended to extend operations of the ISS to 2030. This bill was unanimously approved in the Senate, but failed to pass in the U.S. House.[[416]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nelson-20181020-424)[[417]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-425) In September 2018, the Leading Human Spaceflight Act was introduced with the intent to extend operations of the ISS to 2030, and was confirmed in December 2018.[[418]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-auto-426)[[419]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:1-427)[[420]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-428) Congress later passed similar provisions in its [CHIPS and Science Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CHIPS_and_Science_Act "CHIPS and Science Act"), signed into law by U.S. President [Joe Biden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joe_Biden "Joe Biden") on 9 August 2022.[[421]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-politico-20220809-429)[[422]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Errick_2022-430)

If until 2031 [Commercial LEO Destinations providers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_LEO_Destinations_program "Commercial LEO Destinations program") are not sufficient to accommodate NASA's projects, NASA is suggesting to extend ISS operations beyond 2031.[[423]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-2024DV-431)

### NASA's disposal plans

[![Image 90](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg/250px-Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg)

Many ISS resupply spacecraft have already undergone [atmospheric re-entry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_entry "Atmospheric entry"), such as [_Jules Verne_ ATV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jules_Verne_ATV "Jules Verne ATV").

NASA considered originally several possible disposal options: natural orbital decay with random reentry (as with Skylab), boosting the station to a higher altitude (which would delay reentry), and a controlled de-orbit targeting a remote ocean area.[[424]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ISSEIS-432)

NASA determined that random reentry carried an unacceptable risk of producing hazardous space debris that could hit people or property and re-boosting the station would be costly and could also create hazards.

Prior to 2010, plans had contemplated using a slightly modified Progress spacecraft to de-orbit the ISS. However, NASA concluded Progress would not be adequate for the job, and decided on a spacecraft specifically designed for the task.[[425]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-433)

[![Image 91: International Space Station is located in Pacific Ocean](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Pacific_Ocean_laea_location_map.svg/250px-Pacific_Ocean_laea_location_map.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pacific_Ocean_laea_location_map.svg "International Space Station is located in Pacific Ocean")

![Image 92: International Space Station](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg/20px-Cercle_rouge_100%25.svg.png)

In January 2022, NASA announced a planned date of January 2031 to de-orbit the ISS using the "U.S. Deorbit Vehicle" and direct any remnants into a remote area of the South Pacific Ocean that has come to be known as the [spacecraft cemetery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacecraft_cemetery "Spacecraft cemetery").[[426]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sky-20220201-434) NASA plans to launch the deorbit vehicle in 2030, docking at the Harmony forward port.[[427]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-435) The deorbit vehicle will remain attached, dormant, for about a year as the station's orbit naturally decays to 220 km (140 mi). The spacecraft would then conduct one or more orientation burns to lower the perigee to 150 km (93 mi), followed by a final deorbiting burn.[[428]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-436)[[429]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-437)

NASA began planning for the deorbit vehicle after becoming wary of Russia pulling out of the ISS abruptly, leaving the other partners with few good options for a controlled reentry.[[430]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Nelson-438) In June 2024, NASA selected SpaceX to develop the U.S. Deorbit Vehicle, a contract potentially worth $843 million. The vehicle will consist of an existing [Cargo Dragon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon_2 "SpaceX Dragon 2") spacecraft which will be paired with a significantly lengthened [trunk module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Service_module "Service module") which will be equipped with 46 Draco thrusters (instead of the normal 16) and will carry 30,000 kg (66,000 lb) of propellant, nearly six times the normal load. NASA is still working to secure all the necessary funding to build, launch and operate the deorbit vehicle.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-:2b-15)[[430]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-Nelson-438)

On 20 February 2025, [Elon Musk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elon_Musk "Elon Musk"), CEO of SpaceX and [Senior Advisor to President Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Senior_Advisor_to_the_President_of_the_United_States "Senior Advisor to the President of the United States"), suggested in a tweet that the International Space Station be de-orbited "two years from now" as Musk believes the station has "served its purpose" and has "very little incremental utility". Despite this, no official decisions on moving up the de-orbiting date have been made yet by the president.[[431]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-439)[[432]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-440)

### Post mission proposals and plans

The follow-up to NASA's program/strategy is the [Commercial LEO Destinations Program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_LEO_Destinations_program "Commercial LEO Destinations program"), meant to allow private industry to build and maintain their own stations, and NASA procuring access as a customer, starting in 2028.[[433]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-f400-441) Similarly, the ESA has been seeking new private space stations to provide orbital services, as well as retrieve materials, from the ISS.[[434]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-s075-442)[[435]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-y695-443)[Axiom Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Station "Axiom Station") is planned to begin as a single module temporarily hosted at the ISS in 2027.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sn-20241218-198) Additionally, there have been suggestions in the commercial space industry that the ISS could be converted to commercial operations after it is retired by government entities,[[436]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-trump-budget-request-444) including turning it into a space hotel.[[412]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-q972-420)

Russia previously has planned to use its orbital segment for the construction of its [OPSEK](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_Piloted_Assembly_and_Experiment_Complex "Orbital Piloted Assembly and Experiment Complex") station after the ISS is decommissioned. The modules under consideration for removal from the current ISS included the Multipurpose Laboratory Module (_Nauka_; _MLM_), launched in July 2021, and the other new Russian modules that are proposed to be attached to _Nauka_. These newly launched modules would still be well within their useful lives in 2024.[[437]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-RussiaSave-445) At the end of 2011, the [Exploration Gateway Platform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Gateway_Platform "Exploration Gateway Platform") concept also proposed using leftover USOS hardware and _Zvezda 2_ as a refuelling depot and service station located at one of the Earth–Moon [Lagrange points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_points "Lagrange points"). However, the entire USOS was not designed for disassembly and will be discarded.[[438]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-446)

Western space industry has suggested in 2022 using the ISS as a platform to develop orbital salvage capacities, by companies such as CisLunar Industries working on using space debris as fuel,[[439]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-g664-447) instead of plunging it into the ocean.[[414]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-y941-422)

NASA has stated that by July 2024 it has not seen any viable proposals for reuse of the ISS or parts of it.[[423]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-2024DV-431)

Cost
----

The ISS has been described as the [most expensive single item](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lists_of_most_expensive_items_by_category "Lists of most expensive items by category") ever constructed.[[440]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-zidbits-expensive-448) As of 2010, the total cost was US$150 billion. This includes [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA")'s budget of $58.7 billion ($89.73 billion in 2021 dollars) for the station from 1985 to 2015, Russia's $12 billion, Europe's $5 billion, Japan's $5 billion, Canada's $2 billion, and the cost of 36 shuttle flights to build the station, estimated at $1.4 billion each, or $50.4 billion in total. Assuming 20,000 man-days of use from 2000 to 2015 by two- to six-person crews, each man-day would cost $7.5 million, less than half the inflation-adjusted $19.6 million ($5.5 million before inflation) per man-day of [Skylab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skylab "Skylab").[[441]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-lafleur20100308-449)

In culture
----------

The ISS has become an international symbol of human capabilities, particularly human cooperation and science,[[442]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-w619-450) defining a cooperative international approach and period, instead of a looming [commercialized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercialization_of_space "Commercialization of space") and [militarized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Militarization_of_space "Militarization of space") interplanetary world.[[443]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-v592-451)

### Film

Beside numerous documentaries such as the IMAX documentaries _[Space Station 3D](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space\_Station\_3D "Space Station 3D")_ from 2002,[[444]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-452) or _[A Beautiful Planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A\_Beautiful\_Planet "A Beautiful Planet")_ from 2016,[[445]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-453) and films like _[Apogee of Fear](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apogee\_of\_Fear "Apogee of Fear")_ (2012)[[446]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-huffpost-20120114-454) and _[Yolki 5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yolki\_5 "Yolki 5")_ (2016)[[447]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-ria-20161212-455)[[448]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-456) the ISS is the subject of feature films such as _[The Day After Tomorrow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Day\_After\_Tomorrow "The Day After Tomorrow")_ (2004),[[449]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-shaw2008-457)_[Love](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Love\_(2011\_film) "Love (2011 film)")_ (2011),[[450]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-458) together with the Chinese station [Tiangong 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tiangong_1 "Tiangong 1") in _[Gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity\_(2013\_film) "Gravity (2013 film)")_ (2013),[[451]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-459)_[Life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life\_(2017\_film) "Life (2017 film)")_ (2017),[[452]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-sony-life-460) and _[I.S.S.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/I.S.S.\_(film) "I.S.S. (film)")_ (2023).[[453]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-461)

In 2022, the movie [_The Challenge_ (_Doctor's House Call_)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Challenge_(2023_film) "The Challenge (2023 film)") was filmed aboard the ISS, and was notable for being the first feature film in which both professional actors and director worked together in space.[[454]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nyt-20210916-462)

### Literature

The 2023 novel by English writer [Samantha Harvey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samantha_Harvey "Samantha Harvey"), _[Orbital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital\_(novel) "Orbital (novel)")_, is set in the space station. It won the [2024 Booker Prize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2024_Booker_Prize "2024 Booker Prize")[[455]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-463)[[456]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-464)

[Ceridwen Dovey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceridwen_Dovey "Ceridwen Dovey")'s _Only the Astronauts_, a 2024 collection of short stories in which the narrator in each story is an inanimate object in space, includes the International Space Station.[[457]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-465)

### Video games

The ISS is blown up during the _[Call of Duty: Modern Warfare 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Call\_of\_Duty:\_Modern\_Warfare\_2 "Call of Duty: Modern Warfare 2")_ mission "Second Sun", in which the character Captain Price launches an [ICBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile "Intercontinental ballistic missile") into the earth's atmosphere; the resulting shockwave destroys the station.[[458]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-466)

The ISS is present in _[Far Cry New Dawn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far\_Cry\_New\_Dawn "Far Cry New Dawn")_ as an expedition site, having fallen to earth during a [nuclear war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_warfare "Nuclear warfare").[[459]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-467)

Notes
-----

1.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-105)**_Pirs_ was connected to the nadir port of Zvezda now occupied by _Nauka_.
2.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-106)**partially retracted
3.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-108)**"Zarya" has several meanings: "daybreak" or "dawn" (in the morning) or "afterglow", "evening glow" or "sunset" (in the evening), but NASA and Roscosmos translate it as "sunrise."[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-107)
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-267)**Privately funded travellers who have objected to the term include Dennis Tito, the first such traveller,[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-260)[Mark Shuttleworth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Shuttleworth "Mark Shuttleworth"), founder of [Ubuntu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system) "Ubuntu (operating system)"),[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-261) Gregory Olsen and [Richard Garriott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Garriott "Richard Garriott").[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-262)[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-nbc-20050913-263) Canadian astronaut [Bob Thirsk](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bob_Thirsk&action=edit&redlink=1 "Bob Thirsk (page does not exist)") said the term does not seem appropriate, referring to his crewmate, [Guy Laliberté](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guy_Lalibert%C3%A9 "Guy Laliberté"), founder of [Cirque du Soleil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cirque_du_Soleil "Cirque du Soleil").[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-264) Anousheh Ansari denied being a tourist[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-esa-notatourist-265) and took offence at the term.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_note-spacecom-20060915-266)
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-269)**ESA director Jörg Feustel-Büechl said in 2001 that Russia had no right to send 'amateurs' to the ISS. A 'stand-off' occurred at the Johnson Space Center between Commander [Talgat Musabayev](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talgat_Musabayev "Talgat Musabayev") and NASA manager [Robert Cabana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Cabana "Robert Cabana") who refused to train Dennis Tito, a member of Musabayev's crew along with [Yuri Baturin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yuri_Baturin "Yuri Baturin"). Musabayev argued that Tito had trained 700 hours in the last year and was as qualified as any NASA astronaut, and refused to allow his crew to be trained on the USOS without Tito. Cabana would not allow training to begin, and the commander returned with his crew to their hotel.
6.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-282)**Including the modified DC-1, M-MIM2 and M-UM module transports
7.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-283)**Includes both crewed and uncrewed missions
8.   ^ [_**a**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-Prichal_286-0)[_**b**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-Prichal_286-1)[_**c**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-Prichal_286-2)[_**d**_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-Prichal_286-3)The Prichal aft, forward, port and starboard ports still have their protective covers in place and have yet to be used since the module originally docked at the station.

92.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-nasa-img-ksc-08pd0991_93-0)**Shiflett, Kim (22 April 2008). ["KSC-08pd0991"](https://images.nasa.gov/details/KSC-08pd0991). _NASA Image and Video Library_. Cape Canaveral, Florida. [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20231124185310/https://images.nasa.gov/details/KSC-08pd0991) from the original on 24 November 2023. Retrieved 5 July 2019. In the Space Station Processing Facility at NASA's Kennedy Space Center, an overhead crane moves the Kibo Japanese Experiment Module – Pressurized Module toward the payload canister (lower right). The canister will deliver the module, part of the payload for space shuttle Discovery's STS-124 mission, to Launch Pad 39A. On the mission, the STS-124 crew will transport the Kibo module as well as the Japanese Remote Manipulator System to the International Space Station to complete the Kibo laboratory. The launch of Discovery is targeted for May 31.

252.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-airandspace-201104_256-0)**Hoversten, Paul (April 2011). ["Assembly (Nearly) Complete"](https://www.smithsonianmag.com/air-space-magazine/assembly-nearly-complete-2091210/). _[Air & Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air\_%26\_Space "Air & Space")_. In fact, we're designed on the U.S. side to take four crew. The ISS design is actually for seven. We operate with six because first, we can get all our work done with six, and second, we don't have a vehicle that allows us to fly a seventh crew member. Our requirement for the new vehicles being designed is for four seats. So I don't expect us to go down in crew size. I would expect us to increase it.

["Antares rocket launches heaviest Cygnus cargo ship ever to space station for NASA"](https://www.space.com/northrop-grumman-heaviest-cygnus-cargo-ship-launch). _[Space.com](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space.com "Space.com")_. 277.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station#cite_ref-her_285-0)**Cook, John; Aksamentov, Valery; Hoffman, Thomas; Bruner, Wes (September 2011). [_ISS Interface Mechanisms and their Heritage_](https://ntrs.nasa.gov/api/citations/20110010964/downloads/20110010964.pdf)(PDF). AIAA Space. Houston, Texas: [Boeing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing "Boeing"). [Archived](https://web.archive.org/web/20230810133110/https://ntrs.nasa.gov/api/citations/20110010964/downloads/20110010964.pdf)(PDF) from the original on 10 August 2023. Retrieved 31 March 2015. Docking is when one incoming spacecraft rendezvous with another spacecraft and flies a controlled collision trajectory in such a manner so as to align and mesh the interface mechanisms. The spacecraft docking mechanisms typically enter what is called soft capture, followed by a load attenuation phase, and then the hard docked position which establishes an air-tight structural connection between spacecraft. Berthing, by contrast, is when an incoming spacecraft is grappled by a robotic arm and its interface mechanism is placed in close proximity of the stationary interface mechanism. Then typically there is a capture process, coarse alignment and fine alignment and then structural attachment.
